RATING: 7.5
A worker placement game with a great unique mechanic (gears), and simple options but tough decisions.

Set in the time of the Mayans, this is yet another worker placement game(!), however this time, there are actually some nice new aspects combined with tried and true mechanics.  Your goal is to of course, accumulate the most points by the end of the game, and there are 2 in-game scoring rounds, and a final scoring.  On your turn, your choices appear simple: 1)place a worker(s), or 2) take back a worker(s).  You CANNOT do both even though many times you want to.  Your placements are on moving gears, of which there are 5 of (with a central gear that moves them all), and the longer you leave your worker, the better the payoff.  However, you will need to take them off on occasion simply because you have no more to place- also if you wait too long, they will get nothing.  As well, you have several workers, but the more you place, the more expensive it gets.  

The gears provide: 1) resources (wood) and food/currency (corn), 2) better but more scarce resources (wood, stone, gold, skulls), 3) ability upgrades and construction/buildings and temple sacrifices, 4) more workers and flexible options to use currency, and 5) big point generators where you place skulls.  There are 4 checkpoints in the game where you either score points or collect resources IF you've been good to the gods, and where you have to feed your workers (or lose points like Agricola).  There are several different paths here, you can go crazy building buildings which generate points and give small bonuses and abilities, you can go for skulls which provide big points, you can go heavy on sacrifices for the gods which pay out points 3 times during the game, you can do a bit of everything.  The theme works, and the game scales very well (2-players works great despite the seeding of initial dummy players/blocks).  The gear mechanic looks and plays great, very impressive component implementation.  

Own the [boardgame=143065]Tribes & Prophecies[/boardgame] expansion and the [boardgame=134380]Mayan Calendar - mini expansion 1[/boardgame], [boardgame=142580]Mayan Calendar - mini expansion 2[/boardgame], [boardgame=149801]Tribes & Prophecies - mini expansion 1[/boardgame] promos.

RATING: 8
Tentative first play rating. Loved the game, but concerned about the length (it took us about 4 hours). I think if everyone is familiar with the game and can read the decision space quickly, it might play a whole lot faster. We were definitely speeding up toward the second half of the game. I was surprised to find that the challenging part of the game was not the gears, but calculating when you want to put pieces on and take them off the board and in what combination you want to do it. Corn management was also critical. It feels a lot like the mandala of one of my favorite games, Trajan. You always have to put a worker on the board or take one off, so timing becomes key. Very interesting dynamics & the alternating resource/point scorings really cause ebbs and flows in the game. I also like the variability of the monuments being different each game. This game feels like it would be best if played regularly, which sadly my group does not tend to do. Still, very much recommended.

RATING: 6.5
Der Zahnradmechanismus ist schön. Aber leider verlässt sich das Spiel zu sehr darauf und verschenkt anderweitig sein Potenzial. 
Z.B. die Gebäude und Monumente, die man baut, haben weder Bilder noch Namen, sind einfach lieblos gestaltete Siegpunktplättchen. Zudem spielen sie im Spiel keine weitere Rolle, bringen weder Vorteile, noch spielt die Anzahl/Kombination der Gebäude irgendeine Rolle.
Somit geht zum einen der thematische Anstrich verloren, zum anderen ist es vom Spielgefühl her trocken, nur ein reines Siegpunkt sammeln anstatt etwas aufzubauen.

Ein weiterer Punkt ist, dass man gezwungen ist, sich, auf einen bestimmten Siegpunktpfad festzulegen, und dann ist der Rest des Spiel nur noch Rechnerei, um hier und da noch einen Punkt mehr rauszuholen. Das führt gegen Ende zu Grübelorgien. Es fehlt zudem auch an Spieldynamik und Flexibilität.

Wirklich schade um den tollen Zahnradmechanismus, das hätte so leicht ein echter Kracher werden können.  Für die ersten 1-2 Partien ist es trotzdem spannend, um den Zahnradmechanismus mal auszuprobieren, doch der schlecht konzipierte Rest verdirbt den Wiederspielreiz.

So ist es leider ein Blender, faszinierend auf den ersten Blick, doch dann wird man ernüchtert.

RATING: 6
There isn't anything very new here, except for the cog-turning, which isn't really a new mechanic - just a different presentation of things we've seen before in games like Village - i.e. workers increase their yield the longer they take over their task. The multiple routes to victory, buildings with production costs, and differing victory point generators, all feel like they were pulled straight from Ninjato, or Stone Age, or Walnut Grove, or any of a list of other worker placement games. It's quite a heavy implementation of the genre, and that might appeal to some - certainly heavier than all the afore-mentioned titles (except arguably Village). It looks great, but the theme doesn't really come through. All in all, it's an interesting resource-conversion, multi-player solitaire, puzzle, but there are many other choices if you want a game in this mould.

After a second play, I am even more disappointed in this game. It's a straight-up resource-conversion exercise, with no thematic integration at all. I convert grain into wood, to advance a step in an unspecified technology, which gives me additional wood, allowing me to build an un-named, un-illustrated "building", which gives me more grain. Rinse and repeat. I like dry, relatively themeless Euros, but this really is one step too far, and actually feels a little boring. The vast number of interconnected resources and action-spaces feel indulgent, and the whole thing is unwieldy - similar to another over-hyped, flabby, recent title, Bora Bora. 

I have met many people who like this game, so there is clearly fun to be had here, and I won't turn down future games, but I won't be seeking it out.

DISLIKE: Overwhelming with the number of paths, but the expansion helps give direction with asynchronous powers. Minimal player interaction.

OVERALL: Solid game, I can see why it is well liked. I wish there was a touch more interaction, but I think competing for the different spots is still a good amount. The tension is there.

But the paths are quite fun and the tension is rich - always one short of what you want. It plays fast if folks plan a bit. RATING: 9.7
Euro de mecánica muy original con los engranajes, de colocación de trabajadores y gestión de acciones/recursos a la altura de otros buenos juegos euros, como Caylus.  Escala bien a pesar de los trabajadores neutrales aunque es más interesante jugando con 4. El tema no está mal elegido pero el juego es muy euro puro, muy seco, abstracto y sesudo.
Tras varias partidas te das cuenta que la partida depende de la config inicial tanto de losetas de jugador, como sobretodo de los tipos de edificios, y aun así hay diferentes estrategias por las que optar, dependiendo también del número de jugadores y del desarrollo de la partida: hacerse con los templos, con los edificios, o con las calaveras... etc. por no decir la elección de conseguir trigo o transformarlo, y sobretodo la elección de hacerte o no con más trabajadores, para hacer más acciones, teniendo en cuenta su mayor coste de mantener. Es un jue-ga-zo de sobresaliente de los de calentarte la cabeza de lo lindo. Se juega genial por la BGA y no veo ninguna estrategia ganadora, como se comentó en su día, a raíz de las impresiones de las primeras partidas ;)

RATING: 8.5
After two plays of this game, I've ranked it at #8 on my [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/143773/admiralacfs-game-rankings]list of all-time favorite games[/url].  It's a fantastic brain-burner of a worker placement game.  I love the way the gears are built into the mechanics of the game.  They're not just a gimmick, they really make the game what it is.  The game has a deep level of strategy, but turns move rather quickly because players can make plans while waiting for their turns, and on your turn there aren't so many options as to cause analysis paralysis.

I've played with 3 and 4 players, and the 4 player game does feel a bit more crowded, but I wouldn't say this is any better or worse, just different.

If I had any concerns about the game, it would be with replayability.  There's not a lot that changes from game to game, and the number of age 1 buildings seems rather low, so you could make plans for acquiring a certain building and be pretty sure it'll show up.  This is only a minor concern at this point, and I hope to get this one to the table a lot more.

RATING: 10
This is a solid worker placement game with a unique time management twist and a very well designed board. 

+ elegant and ingenious systems
+ your action choice is very simple: on your turn you either put workers, or take them back
+ simple rules, easy to learn, hard to master
+ very deep experience in a short playing time (two hours, once you know how to handle it)
+ very high replay value 
+ a rather high addiction factor for this type of game: once set on the table you just want to play it over and over again 
+ add the expansion and you'll have a mind-blowing experience. (Endless possibilities, and a very competitive game ... in the same short time.)

- first couple of games might be intimidating, and scare off some players, because the strategies are not obvious the first few games

RATING: 9
A great, "spin" on worker placement, Tzolkin is easily one of my favorite games with the action drafting mechanism. You play workers onto one of several gears. Each gear is turning during play and associated with a family of actions that enable specific strategems in play to be executed. In order to take an action, you must remove one of your placed workers at the right time and position on the said gear. This is the core of the game that all the other mechanisms and options of the game rest on. Other than a fragile board, I find I have nothing negative to say about Tzolkin. I am glad I picked it up at pax east a few years ago and will probably never get rid of it.

RATING: 8
Tzolk'in is a really clever euro-game, that I wanted to try for a long time. My interest was mostly caught by the gears, because the rest of the components, while being high quality, never impressed me for their visual impact. Having finally played it, I can say that those gears are probably a gimmick, but they are also pretty functional to the smooth flow of the game-play.

Speaking of game-play... Based on what I've heard and read on line, I always though of Tzolk'in as a huge brain-burner with a pretty heavy rule-set. When I actually tried it, I was instead surprised by how simple the turns flow in this game. This is not a light game by any mean, but the heaviness of the experience doesn't come from the amount of mechanisms you have to keep in mind in order to play it. The challenge of this game is all about managing your workers and programming your moves many turns ahead... and that's the kind of heaviness that I like.

There's so many things in Tzolk'in that I enjoy: the tech-threes, the tracks of the Gods, the buildings and the monuments. All these elements intertwine perfectly to create a deep and interesting game-play. Yet what really impressed me the most about Tzolk'in is its take on the worker placement idea and its element of time management. I've never played a game before where the time felt so much like a resource. You have to manage your workers in order to meet your goals at the right time and there's even deadlines for which you have to prepare for. While this might be considered stressful by someone - and it probably is - I find it really cool and innovative.

The only complaint I have with about the game is its lack of variety. There's surely multiple strategies you can take, but I just think they don't feel different enough and you can't never actually completely ignore some specific elements, like the Gods tracks for example. Probably this issue could be solved by the expansion, which I'm eager to try. Minor complaint aside though, Tzolk'in The Mayan Calendar is a brilliant strategic game, that has a lot of challenges to offer, and one I'd like to play often given the chance.

RATING: 10
[With the expansion]

I think this is my favorite worker placement game.  It's attractive and the mechanics are both unique and intuitive enough to learn quickly and remember without large time spent re-learning rules.

Nice features:  it's not just solitaire, but not passive-aggressive cruelty, like most worker placement.  You may chance your opponent's plans, but usually won't just take the thing they needed above all else.  Starting player management is actually interesting and fun, rather than an annoying aspect.  Variable setup makes it play different, and most paths are potentially viable.

RATING: 2
Broken Broken Broken. I gave a 2 because it's fun to move the gears.

Why is it broken? I'm surprised you ask. You must not have played a lot with the experts..... What is a broken game? A broken game is a game when ONE AND ONE ONLY AND ALWAYS THE SAME ONE strategy leads to victory. Meaning, if you pursue any other strategy, no matter how good you pursue it and how clever you are, the noob doing the ONE WINNING strategy will outflank you eyes wide shut.

In Tzolk'in case, this strategy is get to 6 workers very fast. Don't feed them by converting wheat to resources. Row 2 level III tech as fast as you can. Pile up, pile up, pile up, pile up like there is no tomorrow. On cycle 4, reap everything, build everything, climb everywhere. and you win with a 200+ score. That's why you don't see any players now doing the skull strategy.

Don't listen to experts who tell you this strategy is counterable. It is not. Unless by the same strategy which proves it is broken. On the contrary, Puerto Rico is not a broken game.

RATING: 7
2-4(4)

The worker placement system is fine enough, but ultimately pretty standard. Really, it's just a form of promoting your placed workers each turn. The gears are just a means of doing that promotion, while also being visually cool.

That's fine. I suppose the big thing about this should be that you can only place or remove workers (and placing costs money based on how many you place). Which leads to some serious planning so that you can consolidate your turns (and not be removing multiple turns in a row, or that you have the resources to execute when you remove).

Another of those games where half the board won't be loaded up by game end (some skulls will be empty, several cult tracks won't be advanced, technologies will not be upgraded, etc.), and thus ultimately sort of just a game to specialize as best you can in certain domains.

Fun enough, but I think others of this oeuvre are more interesting to me, overall.

RATING: 7
I find Tzolk'in a game of two halves.  There is the innovative, engaging half - the cogs - coupled to same old same old resource conversion to score points or gain abilities.

The five cogs each perform a different function.  Most provide varieties of resources, while one of them you go to to make sacrifices and score points. There is a challenging trade-off between the length of time you leave your worker inactive on a cog and the rising pay-off you will receive.  The mechanics of the cogs mean that all the turn by turn adjustments are made with a minimum of fuss - and scoring turns are kept account of. Using only six of 13 monuments each game and, similarly, fewer than the 32 building cards provided, creates variability.  

On the debit side, conversion of resources into technologies and vps seems formulaic and uninteresting.  Player interaction is of the minimal taking stuff before my opponent variety seen so often in efficiency games. 

Production quality is good.  The parts that literally move, fit well together.  Tzolk'in features yet another set of icons and some are opaque to the novice player.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.9
In my opinion, this is a bit over-hyped.  Don't get me wrong--any gamer will find this palatable.  However, aside from the unique gear element (and the subsequent "progressive worker-placement" quality of the game), there's nothing here you haven't seen before.  It strikes me as a hybrid of Agricola and Caylus in terms of the game mechanics.  And, this game is in the same league as those games in terms of complexity as well.  You have the constant concern for food you have in Agricola, along with the ever-present sense that you're barely surviving (if, indeed, you are).  I am reminded of Caylus in terms of the multiple paths to victory, the technology tracks (similar to the favor tracks in Caylus), the buildings you can construct, etc.  This is a game where noobs will get crushed by anyone with experience, which also seems true of Agricola and Caylus.  There are a lot of paths to victory and you have to plan ahead quite a bit; hence, it's a true strategy lover's delight, to be sure.  There's a lot going on in the game and it can be overwhelming at first.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Tentative review because we played this all wrong...
I won the game primarily by currying favor with the gods and earning crystal skulls. The scoring possibilities are dizzying and the wheels aren't just a gimmick. Here is the key to the wheels: they force you to be really careful about timing. You only get to remove your pip or place on a given turn..thus the timing of the wheel almost feels like playing the cymbals in an orchestra: you're waiting for the exact right moment to hit your note.

RATING: 6
Like everyone else, I saw the gears and got really excited about it. Sadly, the calendar gear turning system does not live up to the hype: it is a nice idea to get some excitement into worker placement, but the fact that all the gears (except one) rotate with the same, slow speed made me think about the point of all of it - it really does not do anything new.

But I could have lived with it if the theme would have been interesting. Instead, the Maya topic is reduced to resource conversion and some lazy building and technology system. And it is not even done very well or with any noticable player interaction. All the mechanics feel patched in to add some complexity to the gear idea. But it really takes more collecting wood cubes, corn tokens and developing a 3-space-"take one more" tech tree to make an interesting game. 

I really would have wanted this to work better. :(

RATING: 7
Like many worker-placement euros, players are utilizing their workers to gain resources and then utilizing them to spend those resources in a variety of different ways (Get VP directly, build up their engine, etc). But unlike any worker-placement game before, this game has gears, which is essentially a mechanized version of multiple rondels. See, each time the gears spin, the benefit that the workers get when they are removed becomes stronger (typically). Thus, players must carefully plan the placement and removal of workers, so that they can obtain VP efficiently.

If I had to criticize the game, there seems to be some consensus with competitive players that the 2-player game doesn't have as many viable strategies as the 3/4-player game. That being said, I haven't played the game enough to understand those balance issues. Still, I find the game brain-burning, interesting, and refreshing. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Tzolk'in is nicely constructed.  There are several point scoring routes, the temples seem to be the most important.  The clever part of the mechanism is having to tie up your workers for longer periods to secure the better rewards as they travel round the wheels.  The entry costs onto the wheel are higher of the initial places are full, but this also means you get a better reward or opportunity.  These al feel well balanced.  It is necessary always to plan ahead for your next move and you are likely to fall quickly into a reap and sew routine.  The feeding requirements are taxing and the opportunity for each player to move the cog twice can scupper plans.  Overall a well constructed game I am happy to explore further.  However it is not without its frustrations - someone else buys a temple or tile just as you attempt to.  And I would not call it fun.  A bit like playing a spreadsheet.  One player was caught woefully short of food and penalised.  Unsure if you can be penalised below zero points.  We played not.

RATING: 9.5
A worker placement game with a time component to it, in which the longer your worker stays on the board the better actions he can perform.

On your turn you just have 2 options, either you place workers on the various spaces on the board, and pay for the cost of placing workers, or you take workers from the board and execute the specific actions.

Even though placing several workers on your turn has an increasing cost it is always good to have more than the 3 starting workers because since you always have to place or remove workers, if you only have 3 most likely you'll have to remove some before they reach any significant position on the wheels, if you have several workers you can each turn place only 1 worker and that gives you time to keep your workers on the wheel for longer time.

Too bad it only goes to 4 players. But with the expansion it goes up to 5.

Nagyon jó, hogy a több ember nem jelent feltétlen sikert, az etetéssel ez jól ki lett egyenlítve...

RATING: 8.5
This was my most anticipated game of Essen 2012, and it didn't disappoint.

The heart of Tzol'kin is  of course the worker placement "on wheels": every turn each player must place one or more workers OR retrieve one or more workers and execute their actions.

It seems that there is a lot of different approaches to victory (at least four different "strong" strategies), and it plays very differently than any other (worker placement) game I played: the place or retrieve mechanic has some resemblance with Manhattan Project, but the feeling in Tzol'kin is very different.

The graphic design is superlative: the cogs and puzzle-like board are of course a piece of design, but every information in the game is cleverly depicted on the board or on player mats, so there's no need to ever check the rulebook!
The components are top notch, with only a minor complaint to be made about the fragility of the folding parts of the board.

A great game by CGE and a couple of italian designers!

RATING: 8.45
I am a big fan of Stone Age, and though the mechanics in this game are dissimilar enough to make it unique (not to mention the gears were wicked cool), it was easy to understand and teach my kids.  And everyone insisted on having a chance to turn the gears to move the calendar forward.  A lot of fun and a lot of replayability.

Word of caution, however, about assembly and storage.  The gear assemblies are heavy enough that for the largest section (which includes 2 gears that attach to a board section that folds in the middle to store), the board layers began to separate at the edge.  this was due to the weight of the gears pulling on the board where the sections for the two gears meet while I was attaching the gears for the first time.

I fear that storing this game on its side would aggravate the problem, so would suggest storing it flat.  By the way, I was able to pretty quickly mend the separated piece so that it does not show.

RATING: 8.3
When I saw the box I was excited to see a 90min worker placement game.  With 4 players and about 25 turns that's not going to happen unless everyone plays each and every turn in under a minute.  So definitely this is more 150min+ game.

RATING: 7.25
Okay... So we have an innovative gimmick with the interconnecting rotating wheels and the timing of placing and retrieving your workers, but do we have a good game? I would say that this is a good mid weight Euro Worker Placement game that takes a few games to grok the different paths to victory. It is definitely not a gateway game, but I need to play it a few more times to see if it falls into a gamers game category. Despite the feeling of "been there, done that" with certain aspects of this game, it still manages to hold my attention. 

The production values are good, but the rotating wheels kept getting jammed despite the fixes offered on the publishers website and some of the boards backs began peeling off. Also a minor quibble, the glossy board, despite being pretty, sometimes gets in the way with the glare of the light in certain angles.

Update: Upon playing this a couple of more times, there is more than meets the eye and the layers of depth and strategies are starting to reveal themselves.

RATING: 8
This is definite worker placement with a few different options for scoring points. I was particularly pleased with the fact that the gears appear to be a useful component... they aren't just a gimmick. Food seems to be a common struggle throughout the game. There appear to be several different ways to set yourself up for scoring points and I look forward to playing this one more.

In my second play, I overcompensated for a shortage of food in the first game.  That plus a plethora of other bad choices left me with half the points of first place.  Yikes!  I do need to play this again.

Things started coming together better for me in my third play.  I finished second and was able to pick up two nice scoring monuments.  I still see where I am missing opportunities, after the fact.  I've upped my rating fom 8 to 8.5.

After 9 plays, Tzolk'in is becoming one of my favorite games. I still seem to struggle with it at times, but I love what it presents. There are many different interactive parts to the game, but each turn boils down to either put a guy or guys on the board or take a guy or guys off the board. That's it in a nicely, complex game.  The Tribes & Prophecies expansion just makes it even better.

RATING: 8
Tzolk'in is so pretty, it has a theme (with soundtrack it is awesome) but overall this game is very, very heavy. I mean, rules are simple but it is super hard to build anything in the game to make it works for You, like automatic mechanism for scoring points (game is all about this). With the bad decisions You can finish the game with much about 40-50 points and it is not good at all (experienced players can reach 90-100, it doesn't matter if You have fun playing this game even if You reach only 40) :) When I play this I feel the same like during Le Havre, so many things to do and not enough time. The game also forces You to harvest many corn baskets - it is money and food for workers. No corn or less than 2 baskets means serious trouble and You're actually out of the game for few turns trying to surivive and come back to the game plan. Rotating calendar is cool (1st player is in charge of rotation so everyone want to be first, game forces You to fight for the 1st player marker also :P) but such a shame it is colorless and just ugly :/ they could put cities colors on the specific modes of this calendar. It looks unfinished and dazzle. Finally - this is heavy strategy which can be overwhelming despite the look but definitely deserves to be #22 on BGG. Warning: You have to find right people to play this game with, just saying. Game can be stressful.

3p - IMO better than for 2p (I really enjoyed it)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Es un juego de colocación de trabajadores, que tiene como novedad que los trabajadores se colocan sobre unas ruedas móviles que, en cada turno, al avanzar la rueda central que indica el paso del tiempo y controla la duración de la partida, cambian de posición.

Cuanto más tiempo pase un trabajador en una rueda en movimiento, más potente será la acción que pueda llevar a cabo, pero el jugador sacrifica uno de sus trabajadores durante varios turnos.

En su turno un jugador puede o colocar trabajadores (los que desee) o retirarlos (y entonces se ejecuta la acción).

Es sencillo de explicar, pero dificil de controlar y jugar bien. Hay muchas formas de obtener puntos. Me ha gustado mucho. Es muy original.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Una maravilla de juego, de colocación de trabajadores, en los que la intensidad de la acción es mayor cuanto más tiempo pasa el trabajador colocado (la acción se ejecuta cuando se recoge). 
En cada turno se mueve una rueda central que mueve otras cuatro, que contienen las aciones principales.
Hay muchas formas de puntuar y muchas estrategias posibles.. Es una delicia. 
Totalmente recomendable,eso sí, para jugones.

RATING: 10
I like to play worker placement games, but this one stands head and shoulders above the others I've played. I can't think of anything this game has done wrong, it is so balanced. It offers just the right amount of choices for advancement on the tracks and scoring, that in my first play I felt in control of the game flow. RATING: 7.5
A very fun twist on an otherwise very straightforward worker placement game.

Some people complain that the gears are a gimmick. I think they make it super easy from a bookkeeping perspective to make time a factor in the game. Sure it could be done without the gears, but it would be much more difficult.

The turn structure is simple: Play workers or remove workers. The iconography makes sense at a glance. The hardest part is playing well!

Getting hopelessly behind can be frustrating, but pulling off a perfectly timed, multi-moved worker pull to get everything you need is incredibly rewarding

RATING: 9
Very impressed with this on first play and looking forward to playing again.  Longish rules may make it appear more complicated than it really is; though they can be forgiven their length as they make pretty much everything crystal clear and the iconography on the board does a very good job of reminding you what each of the actions etc. do.  Getting past that first impression though it's clear that the relatively straightforward rules provide the basis for an involved game which delivers on the promise of the excellent presentation (an attractive Mayan setting featuring temples and - of course - the gears) and which really rewards thoughtfull play without causing too much brainburn.  First play was, perhaps inevitably, slowish but I can see it speeding up a lot in future now the basics have been absorbed - you should be able to plan ahead for most turns once the gears have turned; which should make a big difference next time.  

Altogether an excellent first impression, though I'm holding back on an even higher mark for now till that is conifmed - though I have every expectation that it will be and that my rating will increase.  

As an aside can I say how wonderful it is to be living in a world where we in Scotland can play a game designed by Italians (apologies if this is incorrect - I'm going by the names), produced by a Czech company, in an English language version co-produced by a US company and acquired in Germany (at Spiel).  That's an Internationalism I'm more than happy to sign up for.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A cool twist on worker placement.  The gears look really chaotic at first blush, but they're really just a mechanism where you have to wait out your right on the gear until the gear rotates to the action you want.  You start at a crappy action on the gear, and as the gear rotates, the options get better and stronger.  So, the game is about when to jump off and get what you need.

Each gear also is "themed" toward specific parts of the game.  One gear is the harvesting gear, another is an economic gear, etc.  So, you need to place your limited workers carefully to ensure you get the stuff from one gear that you'll need to do stuff on another gear.  Neat timing problems to solve!

There are also several ways to score points, and strategies that revolve around all of them.  Good stuff!

Overall, a really solid worker placement game.  The only drawback is that the game is a bit of a learning curve off the bat, but everything is rather logical once you get rolling.

RATING: 4
An ok game of choosing a path to victory early on (likely based on the cards you drew for initial bonuses and the available monuments) and then going for it.

During the game, like all worker placement games, you'll simply be figuring out what you need to do and what order you need to place your workers in in order to get what you want. The mild difference here is that you'll have to figure out on which turns to place your workers in order to minimize the number of turns where you'll be picking up workers. 

So, while the gears make it look very different than most worker placement games, the dynamic that you experience during play is pretty much identical because you are using exactly the same thought process in order to accomplish goals.

Seriously, just play Agricola.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Tzolk’in is a great, great game! I own more than 200 games and meet with other guys too and play their games, so the fact I played this game 6 times in a January of relatively few plays speaks for itself! 

The wheel isn’t just a gimmick but an integral part of the gameplay and it is a quite interesting and unique element I think. There aren’t a whole lot of ways to score VPs (skulls wheel, god tracks, monuments), but each of my games has played out differently and been won in different ways. It does seem like the Chizchen Itza wheel is quite strong as you also move up on one of the god tracks, but if the other players know this you won’t be the only one to take advantage of this wheel, which suddenly makes the other wheels emptier and the other strategies easier to pursue and perhaps even more powerful… It all evens out in the end. 

The starting player rule is unlike many other games and is a brilliant move by the designers. The way you pass on the starting player marker if you are already the starting player, which you might want to do in order to get the food, is clever. But the biggest advantage of grabbing the starting player is (often) that you can advance the wheel two spaces and really mess up the plans of the other players! I also love the technology track which forms your strategy as does your two starting pieces which you chose in the beginning from four different tiles. The 5 wheels are very unique, and though you might focus on one or two, you’ll often need to place your workers on all of the wheels to succeed. 

BGG says the game is best with 4 players, but I really like the dummy players and think it’s perhaps even slightly better with 3. I had expected the game to produce a huge amount of AP because you might be able to plan several turns forward, but it hasn’t been the case in any of my games, so that’s just yet another plus.

RATING: 8
I have to admit it--I have worker placement fatigue. It's a great mechanic, but so many new games start out with "OK... This is a worker placement game..."

Tzolkin is the exception--it adds something new that is legitimately interesting. The time element of leaving workers on the gears is a legit and fascinating element to the game. Now you are choosing where to place your guys, but also how long you let them hang around for maximum benefit. 

The rest is pretty familiar stuff. Gather resources and food, build stuff, shoot for endgame bonuses... That's not bad as it means the rest of the game fits like a comfortable shoe. I'm interested enough in this to possibly pick up a copy.

RATING: 10
After playing Agricola and falling in love with it, I played Tzolk'in and after one play went out and purchased for my own collection. This game eliminates a lot of the "screwage" factor that is apparent in Agricola. While I love Agricola for this reason, I also love Tzolk'in because it allows you to be on the same wheel with a chance to get the same action that a previous player took. If you move forward too far, you can pay 1 corn per space you need to move back in order to take the appropriate action you were looking for. And, if you happen to get moved to the very last action space, you get a special aciton and can take any of the actions on that particular wheel. I love the fact there is one space on the board that is essentially a free for all. You can pay 1 corn to take any action, as long as it is not on the "skull" wheel (my apologies for not knowing the names of the various gears by heart). I did not win my first game, but came in a close second. As the gameplay was being described, I felt it was a bit much, almost like Caylus, but upon playing learned it was actually much simpler than it sounded. This is a fantastic medium weight game that is not for beginner board gamers, but certainly has a lot of replayability and features that will bring this hobbyist back for more!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Pretty Good and enjoyable!  The gears in the game are more than just eye-catching and gimmick.  The game mechanism presents the timing aspect very well.  Based upon the wheel moving for each round, the worker can increase its production value as long as it is staying on the wheel.  I also like the idea that "no action" is actually one of the actions.  
I believe if you like playing Caylus and Agricola, you will love this game.

The wheel mechanism in Tozlk is much better than "Labora" which only provides the supply of the products.  The gears in Tozlk carry the theme with Commerce, Religion, Architecture and Resources.  Although the theme carrying is a little bit loose, the ways to play on is very innovative.  The gears force players to think ahead and give blocking interactions to other players.  Good 2-3 players games!  

Let me think about the draw back of this game.... it's not easy to find enough food for people.  Also, it may be long waiting to get your crystal skull.  The mechanism may not be easy to pick up for non-gamers at the beginning.  If you lose at the first half of the game, it is hard to catch up.  

The components are top notch.  I give 9.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
There wasn't a gamer who wasn't intruiged by Tzolk'in's gears when the game was presented at Spiel 2012, and I have a strong feeling that the title will hit the top-50 before 2012 is over and done with. A nicely hyped title, therefore.

I too was lured by the gears, but after playing have to come to the conclusion that what I imagined them to be was not what they actually were. I had thought that there would be a fierce battle over position with nasty rotation issues which would see you slide beyond a desired action. Having a lot of workers would make up for the deficiency. In practice, however, the amount of workers you have is quite limited, and you are allowed to execute actions on previous spaces of the wheel provided you pay. It turns a game-theoretical problem of playing other players into simply supplying enough money, and that is a much more 'tame' way of doing things. That said, the mechanism works well, and requires a good judge of timing just the same: the fact that money makes up for a lack of timing doesn't mean that it is [i]easy[/i] to obtain said money...

Unfortunately, the game the gears are connected to is rather run-of-the-mill. It's basically another resource conversion ensemble where resources obtained here become either points now, points in the long term, more abilities in the long term, etcetera. You get the idea: the core is really stock-standard, absolutely devoid of new tricks, and so forth. It works just fine, appears to be well-balanced too, but it's not as novel as the gears driving it; and the discrepancy is a little grating. In other words, the novelty of the game rests solely with said gears, and there I noticed, to my surprise, that after about 2/3rds of playing the game I was getting fed up with them already. It really is, quite literally this time, a mechanical exercise in optimising the production of resources. Again, I'm not saying it is easy to do it right, nor am I saying that matters are broken. I [i]am[/i] saying that the gears only provide a novel selection mechanism for the resources, and that it is hard to see what else the game has to offer. Since the various 'paths to victory' appear to be in order and at least sufficiently diverse, I concluded that the designers had at least given thought to this aspect, and have not let themselves carried away with their gimmick. That's a good thing. But still I was somewhat relieved that the game was done in the end: the gears don't rotate all that quick, and combined with the simplicity of the actions involved, it all becomes a bit of a drawn-out whole which gets older sooner than I wanted it to be.

I again repeat that the resource conversion game fed by the gears appears fine, and I'm not totally deaf to its Siren call. It reminds me, a little, of Amyitis, where such relatively simple paths exist too, but in a much smaller number, and without the sexy gears to drive it. The fact that you now [i]can[/i] purchase earlier actions works in the game's favour at this point. But in order to decide what wins out, my for the moment slight distaste for the gears, or the interest in the multitude of paths at the end, I'd have to play more. That's not going to be a problem since several people in my surroundings have succumbed to the Siren call and handed over their money; as for me, I'm going to listen a bit more before deciding.

RATING: 8.5
Undoubtedly a visual stunner with its artwork, clever wheel mechanism and "crystal" skulls, Tzolk'in is also a very intriguing game with so much to pay attention to, it will leave your head spinning.

Each round players need to place OR take workers, but not both.  That small rule will cause immense anguish as you try to do all you want to at critical times.  Your workers can "ride" a wheel for only so long, and each space they turn to offers different benefits, so that timing is essential.  And will another player decide to advance the wheel twice in a turn, wrecking all your careful plans?

While I loved all the complexity, I do see some balance issues in the game that are of a concern, but not enough to ruin the overall fun.  Definitely a game worth exploring for all the Euro fans out there.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
In short, Tozlk'in represents the pinnacle of the resource-conversion worker placement genre. Far from being just a visually appealing gimmick, the rotating gears constitute a giant puzzle to solve and provide a pretty unique additional dimension in the form of time management. Throw in an attractive theme, simple rules, multiple viable strategies, almost no luck involved and (expansion alert!) variable powers and goals, and the result is a brain-burning gem asking you to constantly plan several turns in advance. It could probably use some more player interaction, but hell I just love it.

RATING: 8
Woah, finally managed to play this one. Moving these gears is so fun and satisfying! I like seeing the progress, how my workers get more and more efficient as the time goes on. Tzolkin really wants you to plan ahead - you must have a very careful plan for subsequent turns and see everything in your head in order to create a powerful synergy between the possible action in one turn. Therefore don't expect high scores in your first play. The very simple main mechanism - either put workers onto the board or take them off, never combine these two - works very well. Later in the game very powerful combos emerge when you take off 4 or 5 workers and do various stuff! Aside from that time system actions are rather typical euroish, get resources, move on the technology/temple track, trade, construct buildings and monuments that give you additional options, also for scoring. There is feeding your workers but it's not overly restrictive and it's easy to fulfill. Game length is ideal - not too short, not too long, you'll have time to do what you want. You only need to wait, wait, wait... and plan carefully. This game punishes for mistakes(damn, my worker went one space too far, damn, I cannot combine these two actions as I'd like and so on...) but is very rewarding when you succeed at merging your actions together efficiently. There's little player interaction - not too much blocking despite it's working placement. Sometimes it happens, but in most cases accidentally and not on purpose. I don't mind that as there's already much to do and analyse! Looking forward to play again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
2-4 players, 90-120 minutes, medium heavy, worker placement

The game looks great, has a nice theme and adds something to the worker-placement genre through the introduction of the cart wheel which turns all the other wheels, so you have to pay attention when you place and take off your meeples. 

As you're only allowed to either place or take off meeples in your turn, timing is probably more important here than in any other game I know. 
An additional factor too add some complexity is the starting player space. When you claim this you receive all the food (corn) which was placed there in previous turns, but also you have the opportunity to turn the wheels twice instead of once, which can seriously mess with other players plans.

Every cart wheel has it's own purpose: food and wood, resources, development and buildings, trade and workers and lastly offerings to the gods. Basically, you need everything and can't neglect any wheel, but the number of workers is very limited and like I said, you can only put workers on one or more wheels or take them off. As the longer they remain on the wheel, the better the reward, so you'd like to keep all of them on as long as possible, which you can't. This part is soooo hard to master, and I'm still learning with every game.

To summarize, this game is highly recommended and has been in my top 10 for a couple of years now.

Can't wait to play again :)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.25
[29 Mar 2017] - 7 (1 play)
I really enjoyed playing this game and had it on my wish list for a long time. There is a lot to think about when placing workers - do you want to pay more for a spot at a space and how long are you willing to let the worker wait before collecting your goods? I found the time element, having a worker occupied on the gears, another constraint that you must constantly deal with. After a year since my play and the game never rising up to get purchased, I dropped my initial rating that this was an 8 and put it at a 7. It's one I'll play if it's the right time but I'm not seeking out plays or even compelled to buy it.

[29 June 2017] -  7.25 (2 plays)
Finally played this again with 4 people. I like the tight board and the additional dimension of time that is added with the gears. A very solid worker placement game that has more to think about than just getting to a spot first. I still find it hard to get the efficiency engine going and thinking that's just me not having my timing down on when I want to take the first player marker so I get better deals on placement spots. The one flaw to this game may be that it's dry. Most of the time it's just about value of spots on the board and converting resources. The theme is cool but doesn't really add to the game play and it could just as well be abstract symbols.

RATING: 8
This is my review after having played 6 two player games and 1 4 player game.

The good

- Fun, challenging, strategic and hard to master.

- Nice variation to worker placement, even though it is quite similar to Caylus.

- With 2 players it plays rather quickly (less than 1.5 hours).With 4 however it is more like 2.5 to 3 hours, which I find longish.

- Equally good with 2 to 4 players. The game scales very well because there is so little interaction.

- Low downtime because it is possible to think ahead.

- Quick to set up and clean up.

- There is almost no bookkeeping to be done during the game, thanks to the gears.

- The rules are not too complex, not too fiddly and mostly intuïtive.

- The crystal skulls look nice.

- Good rulebook and good in-game documentation.


The bad

- Multi-player solitaire. There is very little interaction between the players (unlike Caylus, Agricola and Le Havre, where you constantly need to think about what the other players will do).

- The luck factor is low but existent. It stems from the random 4 tiles every player draws at the beginning of the game, the random selection of the start player, the random replenishment of the buildings and the random replacements of the buildings in the second era. You can get lucky if a building you can really use comes available at the right time, or with good start tiles.

- The cogwheels look very uninspiring (although you can make them look absolutely stunning if you are willing to spend time and money on a paint job), and the board is not that beautiful either. Apart from the crystal skulls, there are only cubes 'n discs

- The cogwheels were bent in my copy, so I had to spend 2 hours of boiling and bending to flatten them.

- The theme is rather shallow (but not totally abstract either).


Conclusion: the total lack of player interaction and existing luck factor keep me from giving this game the highest rating.

RATING: 7
The innovation here is the timing of your actions. You place a meeple onto a wheel and then decide how many cranks of the wheel to permit before you take an action by removing the meeple. The actions get better the longer you wait, but you may not be able to hold out for long with your likely mounting need for resources and currency.

The rest of the game is fairly conventional worker placement, with the usual types of decisions and with the usual angst created by your wanting to do far more than you're able to.

RATING: 8.75
I'm not a big fan of worker placement games but this one manages to be both interesting and rather fun. 

What you are trying to achieve is nothing different to any  number of other games, getting VP's by accumulating resources with which to build things and ascending various victory tracks,  but the cogs add a timing element to the 'place and return' worker action selection which does add a very nice dynamic. You are almost trying to spring timed combos of actions to most efficiently use the number of limited turns. 

Lot's to think about although what you can do each turn is very simple - either place worker(s) or activate worker(s).

It also scales well - played with 2, 3, 4 and 5 (with the expansion) and all work well.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
After one play I was pretty sure this was a game I wasn't going to like.

After 7 plays I now consider Tzolk'in to be a very good medium-heavy Euro which I enjoy playing.

The reasons I didn't originally think this would be my kind of game was it's relative complexity, length of play especially with several newbies, possibility of analysis paralysis, and the relatively themeless nature of the game.

Now, with more expert players, a better understanding of the mechanics, strategies, and timing I think this game is just great.

One of the few games where my initial impression was wrong.

RATING: 8
Players are Mayans, acquiring resources and trying to turn them into victory points, using an interesting timing mechanism.  Players on their turn have two options, either place workers onto the game board, and pay corn for the placement.  Alternatively, they can retrieve workers off the game board, earning the actions from the retrieved pieces.  Simple!  Not so fast... the workers are placed onto one of five "cogs", each geared to a central cog, which controls the five action cogs.  At the end of each round of player turns, the central cog advances a notch, moving the five action cogs (and their workers) up a space, onto a more lucrative action.  The idea is to wait for the pawns to reach the action you want on the five cogs, and then retrieve your workers.  

The five cogs each have a different theme - one earns wood and corn, another more general resources, one is dedicated to receiving crystal skulls for favour of the gods, and so on.  There are also technology trees to advance along, buildings to construct, both of which allow you to become more efficient.  There are also three god tracks to climb, for victory points and more resource rewards.

Simple game to play, but wow, very deep in terms of planning and executing the plan efficiently.  The game looks amazing - lovely art, glossy laminated board (to protect it from the moving cogs, I assume), and really good resource management game play.  CGE does it again - stunning game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is clearly a great game, that much can be told from reading reviews and a single play. I definitely want to play more of these to see if the 8 I gave it is justified.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Gorgeous game and probably better than I give it credit for. I am an avowed hater of worker-placement games, but I admit that this one is the best of the bunch thus far.

There are so many opportunities to place people in time that you are rarely ever blocked out of a place. If the free space is blocked, you pay up and are compensated by being advanced ahead of the free space.

The number of scoring methods are staggering, and many plays will be required to wrap my head around the paths to getting ahead. Innovative!

RATING: 10
I resisted buying this for some time, but I was a dummy.  I think this game may be the pinnacle of the worker placement genre for me.  It has many things I enjoy and nothing I don't:

* Variable starting resources that you can select based on initial board setup
* Variable in-game goals (even more so when you add in the expansion)
* Trade-off between cashing in now and waiting.
* Quick turns
* Game lasts long enough to bring plans to fruition, or even weather a mistake to tow (but not too long!)
* Useful technology advancement
* Area majority competition on the temples
* Both specialization and diversification appear to be viable strategies if you remain focused on your objectives
* Lots of things to do, lots of ways to do them, but only a few things give points
* The gears eliminate a lot of error-prone manipulation of pieces
* The expansion makes the game even more dynamic in very interesting ways
* Corn

My rating takes the excellent expansion into account.  Tribes & Prophecies injects a host of dynamic elements into the game, a rare accomplishment for games in this genre.  I'd have to shave off a point without the expansion.

I am tempted to give this game + expansion a 10, but I can't yet bring myself to do so with a cube-trading game, no matter how excellent.  However, I will easily call this game best-in-class (although Carson City might give it a run for its money after I've played that game some more).

UPDATE 2017:  Played this again after 3 years. I can't believe I haven't played this for that long.  It's still fantastic, and with the expansion, it is in fact a 10, even with just two players.

RATING: 6
A very analytical worker-placement game with a fair amount of blocking/screwage.   The main attraction are the plastic gears which work pretty well to advance workers along time tracks.   The game is a chore to teach because so many spaces and buildings have to be explained.   With AP players this would be brutal, especially in the mid-game.    It features three prestige tracks, which is a mechanism that I'm not too fond of (same issue I have with Terra Mystica).  It's such an abstract form of competition.   The Mayan flavor comes through pretty well here although I'd hesitate to call the game thematic.   The gears beg to be painted, and some folks have done a gorgeous job of that, but you have to wonder if the game will be played enough to justify it.   If you like very analytical, "thinky" competition, this game could be a winner for you.

RATING: 6
Tzolkin introduces some nifty new ideas with its trademark gears, but is otherwise not that exciting.  There's nothing wrong with the game, it's just another mid-heavy action-management resource-conversion game, and there's only so many of those I can take in an evening.  

I still need to play it a couple more times to get the hang of things.  Each time I try to get ahead of the curve, but it feels like the game ends a few turns before I can do the stuff I need to.  
I like the usual different paths to victory, but don't feel like they're necessarily balanced.  Some of them are going to be incentivized because the corresponding wonder is in the game at the start, so you can plan for that.  But while most of the wonders only give you points for your progress in a given area, some of them count things done by all players, meaning that there's little reason to do more than dabble in those areas.
I really dislike the fact that someone can take start player and arbitrarily accelerate the game, screwing with everyone else's plans & decision-making.  There's few enough turns in the game already without reducing that number by 4 if everyone spins the wheel once.

RATING: N/A
I think this is a unique game and thus worth owning for everyone's collection. The gears are something new (not entirely new as 01 also uses gear) and innovative in it's own way. 

[b]Update (18th Sept 2013)[/b]: After 10 recorded palys, I'm starting to think that this game boils down to who's best on the three god temple tracks. Simply ignoring this track will caused major lack in point scoring. The VPs you get from these temple tracks are huge to a point that I going all out here will win you the game. Going high and scoring the bonus from the temples is much superior than players who own plenty of buildings, monuments and high on the tech track. You CAN 100% ignore the tech track, going up high on the temple track and win the game with no problem. Because of this, I'm somewhat think this game's only focused is at the temples, thus making it less fun. [b]Update[/b]: Now I honestly think that the expansion fixes this problem as it shifts your focus on other areas to avoid the prophesy penalties in the expansion.

RATING: 9
A game I've heard can get pretty deep and layered. But for our casual game group we've had a great time with whatever strategies we've developed.

A great worker placement game with a unqiue play style based on a wheel that moves every round ticking time away.

It seems so simple either you're moving your workers on the wheels or taking them off, but the timing as to when and how many is what can make this game so much fun (and can get to that way deeper and layered ways can be played).

Can feel pretty punishing always being under the knife trying to feed your workers in time. But that also adds to the excitement.

Good game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.45
One the best worker placement games I had a chance to play. It's probably because of ingenious gear mechanics representing Mayan calendar. Huge wheel in the middle of the board is rotated by one step at he end of every round which causes 5 other smaller gears to rotate together with it, advancing workers placed there to better action spaces. The more your worker waits on the gear, the better the benefit he will get. On first glace it seems complicated but it really is simple to medium one. Still it offers a lot of fun, especially since I love worker placement, resource management Euro games.
Also, I am looking forward to playing it with Tribes and Prophecies expansion.

[b]Update:[/b] Rating modified according to a custom rating system.
[b]Update:[/b] Rating lowered to 8.45 from 8.59 due to low thematic mechanical significance.

[b]Favorite mechanism:[/b] Definitely gears. Nowhere I saw such a fun mechanic to place workers and collect their products.

RATING: 9
Nos megvolt az idei (2012) esseni újdonságok slágerének (legalábbis ez áll a legelőkelőbb helyen a BGG-n köztük) számító játék kipróbálása.
A játékról:
A tábla és az elemek gyönyörűek.
A játék folyamatos tervezést igényel, konfrontáció -tényleges konfrontáció- kevés van benne: ugyan nyilván előbb teszel 1-1- fogaskerékre és ezzel elveszed a lehetőséget mástól, de mivel azért többnyire látható-sejthető ki merre akar elmozdulni, mit tervez (persze ebben lehet tévedni), ezért nem annyira meglepetés többnyire ha kilöknek, elveszik a helyet, elemet.
A fogaskerekek zseniálisak, és nagyon jól kell látni, h mi h merre fog majd jönni. De jól is látható.
A többiek szerint több stratégia is jól működik, az biztos, h a többszöri játék nagy előny az átlátásban: csúnyán levertek, gyakorlatilag esélyem se volt.
Én úgy látom -igaz 1 játék alapján- h a monumentek, a nagy művek igen erősek, egy olyan megszerzése valszeg kell a győzelemhez (de ezt a játékostársak cáfolták, nyert már úgy vki, h nem volt ilyenje. hm, lehet, de szerintem jó eséllyel kell hozzá, persze nem feltétel, de ha jól jönnek hozzá a cuccok, érdemes belevágni, főként 1-2 sok pontosra ráfeküdni. Persze a merev ragaszkodás nem cél, sőt ...). Nagyon fontos, és ezért előny a több játék, h a kezdeti vagyonodból mit lehet kihozni, mire-merre érdemes elindulni, ezt jól kell érezni.
Én ugyebár Caylus fan vagyok, az a top one, még mindig.
Nos ahhoz hasonlítva: erősen közelíti, talán el is éri azt a szintet nálam, de ez több játék alapján fog kiderülni.
Pl, h mennyire lehet stratégiát váltani út közben, mennyire vannak menekülő utak, ha látod más is afelé megy, és nem fogod elérni a terved.
Azt hiszem ennek a játéknak van esélye megszorítani, még ha nem is dönti le a csúcsról a nagy kedvencem.
A kerekek nagyon jól összerakottak, nagyon jól tervezhetőek, ha elsiklasz vmi felett, nincs baj -csak idő persze, egyrészt a kerék végén bmelyik visszább lévőt, másrészt pénzért (kukoricáért) is lehet visszább lévő fogaskereket használni. A kerekeken bár nyilvánvalóan egyre erősebb dolgok vannak, de ez nem feltétlen azt jelenti, h a legerősebb megszerzése a legjobb. Sőt. Gyakran egy olcsóbb jó ütemű "elvétele" többet ér...
A munkások is jól kitaláltak: vagy felteszel vagy le, de vmelyiket kötelező...épp ezért nem feltétlen nyerő a sok munkás, bár van haszna, és nyilván az is egy jó stratégia, de kevés munkás hatásos-jó felhasználása, keveset de sokszor markol legalább olyan jónak tűnik.
az élelmezést nem szent grál,azaz simán lehet hagyni, h 1-1 alkalommal 1 v 2 főt ne etess meg, ugyan mínusz pont, de lehet, h ezzel több pontot szerzel. Ez különösen a játék utolsó értékelő körére igaz. (egy nem etetés -3 pont cserébe mondjuk veszel egy "nagy mű" lapkát, ami meg ad pl. 15 pontot....)
A szabály világos, és egyértelmű nagy kérdések nem merültek fel benne első olvasatra, majd ha lefordítom teljesen kiderül, h tényleg így  van-e

RATING: 6
My initial experience with this game was also played with the Tribes & Prophecies expansion, so it's hard for me to comment on the base game.

That being said, I can imagine enjoying the game much more without the two elements this expansion brings to it, but I'm not sure by how much more.  I found the prophecies to be extremely punishing to those already struggling and solely responsible for creating a runaway leader.  The tribes were a nice touch in terms of making everyone's turn unique.

Our game also had a slight case of AP at the table, which didn't help perception, but I suppose a little can be expected with a game that has so many options available and not necessarily any of the outcomes playing out as expected.

At any rate, the game is a solid worker placement game and the gears are a unique mechanic, but while I am more than happy to play someone else's copy, I don't believe this or the base game will enter my collection any time soon, despite the novelty of the board.

RATING: 8
Haven't played this one enough to have a good feel for the strategy.  At it's heart, Tzolk'in is a worker placement game--the main innovation is (obviously) the gears.  Despite how it might look, the gears aren't just a gimmick--they really influence the strategy and gameplay.  Instead of placing workers where you want them to be now, you need to figure out where they will be when you want to take them off the board--in several turns.  Very eager to play this one a few more times.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Tzolk'in actually reminded me a bit of Trajan. I think the main reason is that both games have a very thought provoking central game mechanism. One thing I thought Tzolk'in handled really well compared to other worker placement games is more workers does not equal more win. If you don't have the means of supporting extra people, they are not worth your investment. This could be argued for Agricola and other games as well, but in Tzolkin not only do you have to feed your people at regular intervals, but just doing the normal worker placement thing will also likely cost you food. This game also seems to have decent variety since each player will have asymmetrical starting resources and not every monument will be used every game. Overall, I thought it was pretty good and look forward to future plays.

-------------Original rating 20/05/17 Score:6.5
[b]Rating based on – 5+ plays, 2 player -3 player[/b]
Set in a Mayan civilization, this is a farming oriented worker placement game where you co-ordinate many actions among an ingenious system of various rotating gears in order to score the most points by game end. 

Everything looks really overwhelming at first and it has a lot of moving parts (literally!) but in essence it's really easy to understand. On your turn you either place workers or collect them and any goods they have accumulated  on the spot where they currently stand. The longer you leave them out working for you the more productive they will be, but you are constrained by a limited workforce in the beginning and you need to maximise your actions as you only have a 28 day timespan to complete the game. The rulebook was easier to follow than I expected, the components are well done and the crystal skulls are awesome!

I bought the game a couple of years ago because there's nothing else quite like it (gears in a board game!) and also because of recommendations, but it remained unplayed because it looked so complicated. Since then I've come to realise that I don't really like worker placement games and having finally played this month I find that Tzolk'in is the antithesis of the ones that I [i]do[/i] like. While it is interesting, it lacks direction so whenever you play you decide whether you're going for a skull placing/corn denial/resource grabbing/temple racing/building & monument strategy and see how it shakes out in the end and the onus is on you to make the most points rather than having to work towards something specific. It's perfect information which promotes cutthroat tactics which I can't stand (“Oh you're low on corn and a food day is coming up? Oh well...then I'll just use every worker to block all of the spaces on the agriculture wheel and take the first player spot just for insurance”) :yuk: For both of those reasons above it's easy for somebody who has mapped out the most efficient way every time to do exactly the same thing for the win rather than vary their strategies. Also, while it uses a very clever way of assigning dummy workers to take up WP spots on the gears in a 2 player game, having played the game at the 3 player count those placements look unnatural and illogical now. Rewards long term planning strategies which is not my forte as I prefer to be a tactical player. My worker placement games of choice remain Walnut Grove and Yedo.

The unique gear system with the incredibly detailed central board, the fact that painting the entire system of gears was my very first painting project, and the bonus of being Pic of the Week (Geek Weekly #167) as a result of that painting are the only real reasons this is a keeper in my collection. Maybe I'll come to appreciate it more somewhere in the future way down the road, but as it stands at present I could take it or leave it.
[b]Status: TRADED for Kingdom Builder Bundle (Kingdom Builder, Nomads, Crossroads, Marshlands, Capitol, and The Island)[/b]

RATING: 9
2012 Secret Santa.

Very interesting. Appealing graphics. Some excellent variations on familiar mechanics. Lots of fun decisions. I especially like the degree of choice of starting resources, a little touch which sets the game off on the right foot for me. Multiple strategies available, certainly more than meet the eye; Chichen Itza, Maximum Corn, Monuments, and Temple dominance.

Very tense with 4 players, almost unpleasantly so. An intense experience, not unlike Twilight Struggle. Competition for actions can be brutal, especially when Food Day approaches. Actions have to be chosen well in advance and contingencies put in place in case they are unavailable.

Winning against competent opposition requires a single minded pursuit of a strategy chosen at the very beginning of the game.  

Provisionally rating this an 8. Also plays well with 2.

RATING: 9.375

- the twist on worker placement
- the pressure of planning ahead perfectly
- the multiple strategy paths
- the artwork
- the "paintability" of the gears
- that there is replayability from exploring strategies
- the asymmetric start to the game
- that the theme does come through due to the solid artwork and visual design of the game

I dislike:
- that there seems to be one or two dominant strategies that are hard to beat (see BGA's Expert players)...
- the thinness of some of the cardboard components--it works, but it's so weird

It's a game I can get better at with repeated play and one that can really reward exploring new strategies. And while the gears mechanism can certainly have another theme pasted over it, the design and artwork of the board being so 100% focused does sell me the sense of theme that the game is going for. I absolutely enjoy the whole puzzle that this game offers.

RATING: 9.5
Fun worker placement game that has an interesting element of programming actions/timing. Each turn you either place 1 or more meeples or remove one or more meeples from several wheels that provide greater benefits the longer you are on them. You may not pass. If you have no meeples to place then you must remove at least one. When you remove a meeple, then you get the reward it occupies on the wheel. Then THE GEAR TURNS and all the wheels rotate a space. 

So, it's vital that you plan your moves such that you have the resource you need from one wheel, at the time when you will be removing your worker from a wheel that requires that resource to purchase something. But oh no, you didn't allocate your workers correctly and now you have to remove one early before you have the resource you need. Or shoot, you want to remain on the wheel for science (because you're STEM minded), but you need to feed your people. (Every quarter you have to feed your people, and there are some mid game scoring/resource collection moments as well).

Additional tension is added by resources becoming scarcer as the game goes on.  There's a LOT going on. But everybody had fun, and it felt like there were many roads to success.

Did I mention you get to turn a gear each round that turns all the wheels?  Okay, I know it's wrong to love a game because you get to turn a gear . . . but oooooh!

I've heard that some people consider the game broken because one of the wheels is superior.  So far, when we've played with the max players, the fight for resources has kept anyone from dominating that way (which I won't spoil here -- google it yourself). I really like this game a lot.

One complaint, some of the iconography is either small or difficult to decipher, and the middle wheel isn't painted (minor issues).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Really nice game with pretty straightforward rules and a good clear rulebook.
On your turn you either put workers on or take workers off and perform the action they were stood next to. Simple enough.

There is a lot of symbolism here (they made the board language independent) and so there is a lot of checking what they all actually mean.

The board has a really shiny finish which whilst it looks nice is not that practical when you are playing if there is even the slightest bit of sunshine. 

It is very reminiscent of agricola in that you are trying to build an engine with the buildings you buy but at the same time you have to keep a stockpile of corn to 'feed' your workers at several points in the game. However it is really quite slow, resources are difficult to acquire in any number and as there are monuments that you can build that cost of lot of resources you can spend the whole game just trying to acquire the resources  build a single monument.

The starting advantages that you get are really nice and give re playability. Next time I pick I will definitely look for more resources on the two start tokens rather than sheer number of corn.

The gears are novel and they do rotate well (unlike 011 which requires unwinding every so often) but it is a bit of a gimmick. All there are is essentially a turn timer.

RATING: 8
A complex game built around a very simple core mechanism: you either place workers on or you take them from the gears. The board is beautiful and entrancing and looks like a toy on the table. Very intimidating at first too. The game scales surprisingly well from two to four players. It has a quirky cost function, which makes placing workers more expensive than feeding them. But it is the most perfect representation of the time value of money which I have yet seen in a board game. It could easily be re-themed as a game about high finance and private equity investments.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Played 1 time with 4 players for 2.25 hours.

Well...um...it's certainly a Euro. 

Actually, this game is noteworthy to me for exactly one reason: it's the title that (for me) killed the Euro. I see the cool dials and I think "Neat-O!" then I look at the rest of the board: VP opportunities everywhere, "tech" tracks, "buildings"...and I get a sinking feeling. 30 minutes into the game I kept thinking "Point salad. I get it. I get why Trashers hate Euros."

And as the game wore on, and AP increased, and as I thought about the high ranking this game currently holds on the Geek (#16 as I write this), I realized that Euros don't seem to be going anywhere. Worker placement; abilities that flip resources into other resources, or into resource/VP abilities, or resource/VP bonuses, or VPs...

I've done this all before. And it's not interesting any more. And the wheel, cool idea in theory, was a minor novelty that kept my interest for about 30 minutes. 

Tzolk'in broke the Euro. I'm glad others like this title, but after growing more and more dissatisfied with "pure" Euros, I think I'm nearly done with them. When they are simpler and play in 30-45, they seem to work for me, but Tzolk'in is - to my mind exactly what's gone wrong with Euro games - and with it's high ranking, I think it can be fairly said that my gaming tastes have been diverging for quite some time.

If you are a Euro lover, enjoy this game, it has everything you love. Every thing. All those things. Those same old things...

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
There have been only a few games in my history of modern board gaming that have absolutely "wowed" me when I first played them: Settlers of Catan (my first real Euro), Caylus, Hansa Teutonica, and now Tzolk'in.  Yes, I had heard all about how the gears were either just a gimmick or were essential to the game depending on who I listened to, and had high expectations coming into my first play.  But these expectations were actually exceeded.  Now I like abstract games just fine, and I can see how this game might not impress people who need theme.  What do I like about this game?:
- The gears are cool, actually save time and help significantly reduce the fiddliness factor, but this game would be pretty much as great without them.  
- There are all kinds of ways to get points and so much good stuff to do with only a hope of doing a fraction of it all.  I was surprised by the sheer number of options available once the various buildings, technology tracks and ways to gather resources are taken into account.
- Each game can play out differently depending on which monuments are randomly dealt out at the beginning of the game.
- Each turn is divided into small bite-sized chunks, making this game fit into my personal "sweet spot" of just the right granularity level.
- Game length can be somewhat controlled by the players.
- It plays great with 2, 3, or 4 players.
- The production quality is nothing short of superb.

I don't really care for the theme, but it's easy to ignore, and the mechanics fit decently with it.  If you love worker placement, you HAVE to at least try this game.

RATING: 9.5
Tzolk'in: the Mayan Calendar is not just another worker placement game, and if you thought that, well, let itself prove you wrong.

This is the first game, of all the ones I know, that adds a truly dynamic aspect to the worker placement genre: to succeed on this game, you have not just to figure out where to place your workers (as in Agricola, Caylus or the majority of games ot this genre), but also when to place them, and to develope the ability of visualize, beforehand, the outcome of its future actions (anticipating even 7 rounds before!). 

Another great achievement of Tzolk'in is that the theme doesn't feel "artificial" or "superfluous", as sadly happens with a lot of "euro-style" board games. In fact, in my opinion, Tzolk'in's success lies in that its core mechanic and its theme are one and the same: the passage of time! Here, the wheels mechanism isn't just a "glossy" and unnecesary element, it's the opposite: they're the soul of the game, the part that, literally, moves the game!

On top of all the previous, I'm pretty confident to say that Tzolk'in offers a rich, deep, rewarding and enjoyable game experience; a highly strategical, but at the same time tactical, game with tons of replayability.

Plus all the mentioned strong points, its production quality is tremendous: a phenomenal artwork + one of the more beautifull gameboards ever designed + top-notch components...

What else can I say? In my top ten board games list, Tzolk'in has earned a fixed place.

Very, very close to perfection!
