RATING: 6
Kicsomagolás, összeszerelés, és egy rövidke próbajáték után az érzéseim:
- A játék sokkal szebb, mint gondoltam. A táblán csodálatosan részletes a grafika, maga a tábla meglepően csillogós. Nem kell megijedni, nem gagyin csillog, hanem szépen. Gondolom a kerekek koptató hatása miatt kellett ez a bevonat rá. Minden token szép, letisztult, érthető. A fa elemek átlagosak, a mechanizmus miatt nem nagyon lehetett díszíteni őket, így meg kell elégedni a henger alakú munkásokkal, és korong jelzőkkel. A kerekek elég jó minőségű műanyagból vannak, nem az a gagyi kopogós fajta. Egy negatívum: a dobozra kívülről rá van ragasztva egy matrica, miszerint mielőtt összerakod a táblát menj fel az megadott weblapra. A weblapon kiderül, hogy a végső tábla nem teljesen olyan méretekkel készült, mint amire számítottak, így a kerekek tengelyének szánt lukakon kb. 0,5 millimétert tágítani kell. Így az első játék előtt számítson mindenki egy kis barkácsolásra, mert különben szorulni fognak a kerekek, tekerésnél le fognak potyogni a bábuk. (Én végül egy megfelelő méretű ceruza segítségével tágítottam, és egy teknősös kulcstartó fejével lapítottam a megfelelőre a lyukakat )
- A szabálykönyv szintén nagyon szép. Részletes, minden példákkal, képekkel van illusztrálva, sőt még két teljes példakör is van benne. Nem kell megijedni a 16 oldaltól, nem bonyolult, azért hosszú, mert minden részletesen le van írva, és illusztrálva.
- A rövidke próbajáték után nagyon izgalmasnak érzem a játékot. Rengeteg fejvakarós szituáció tud adódni. Nem egyértelműen olyan nagy előny a sok munkás, mint sok más játékban, mivel a lehelyezésük pénzbe (gabonába) kerül. Minél többet teszel le egy körben annál drágább. Viszont ugyebár vagy felveszel munkásokat, vagy leteszel, így ha apránként teszed le őket, akkor köröket vesztesz, ha meg egyben mindig mindet leteszed és mindet felveszed, akkor egy vagyon költesz erre. Fogalmas sincs egyelőre, hogy hol van az egyensúly.
Érdekes még ez kerékre lerakósdi is, mivel messze nem csak arról van szó, hogy minél tovább van lent a munkás annál több cuccot hoz. Általában egészen eltérő dolgok vannak egymást követő fogakon, így nagyon jól kell időzíteni a felszedést, hogy ne egyesével kelljen őket szedegetni. Ráadásul gonosz módon, muszáj leszedned, ha nem tudsz letenni munkást, így egyértelműen szívhatsz egy-egy rossz időzítéssel, mert idő előtt le kell szedned a kerékről a figurát, és kapsz mondjuk egy kő helyett egy fát, amire nincs is éppen szükséged.
Ami még érdekes a kerekekben, hogy mindig a legelső még szabadon lévő pozícióra lehet letenni munkást. Így aki később tesz nincs egyértelműen hátrányban, mert ugyan kell neki plusz gabonákat betenni, viszont egy kör alatt eljuthat oda, ahová mások 3-4 kör alatt. Megintcsak marhára jól kell időzíteni, és a ellenfelek játékát olvasni.

Összességében egy gyönyörű és számomra nagyon ígéretes játéknak tűnik. A szabály a hosszúság ellenére a játék nem bonyolult. Mondjuk nem egy családi, de azért normális ember és geek között félúton lévő barátainknak is meg lehet tanítani.

Szerdán gémbe tervezem levinni, meg lehet csodálni, érdeklődés függvényében ki is lehet próbálni.


szerk:
Még egy-két gondolat, ami eszembe jutott:
- A próbajáték alapján szerintem két játékossal is nagyon jól működik. Ilyenkor a kerekeken bizonyos pozíciókat fixen le kell blokkolni, de ez ugyebár a tekeréssel folyamatosan odébb tolódik.
- Egy negatívum az alkatrészekkel kapcsoltban: a nyersanyagok kicsit gyengébb fény mellet már nagyon nehezen megkülönböztethetőek. Főleg az arany és a fa keverhető.
- Mechanizmusban még, ami izgalmas, de nem írtam:
1. jópofa, hogy nem ugyanazokkal a nyersanyagokkal kezdenek a játékosok, hanem 4 véletlenszerűen osztott indulólapkából választanak kettőt az játék elején és ez alapján kapnak nyersanyagokat, búzát, pozíciókat a technológiai skálán és a templomokban. Így nincs az, hogy mindenkinek ugyanaz kell a játék elején, illetve minden játék első pár köre ugyanaz.
2. Az újrajátszhatóságot a monument nevű épületek biztosítják. A játék elején kiteszünk véletlenszerűen 6 ilyen épületet. Ezek nagyon drágák, és csak a játék végén érnek pontokat. Hasonlóan a stone age-hez (egiziához), olyasmik után kapjuk a pontot, mint a munkások száma, megépült ilyen-olyan épületek száma stb. De a fentiekkel ellentétben itt minden játékban csak ez a 6 áll rendelkezésre, nem jön fel új, nem pörög végig a pakli, így minden játékban más és más dolgok azok, amikkel nagyot lehet szakítani a végén. 

---
Az a fő baj ezzel a játékkal, hogy nem különösebben szórakoztató. Olyan képességeket tornásztat meg, amiben egy kétsoros program is sokkal jobb az embernél. Meddő felhasználása a szőlőcukornak.
->6.9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
If it weren't for the giant turning cogs I think Tzolkin would run the risk of being easily written off as yet another overloaded worker placement game. Even so, aren't those wheels just a big gimmick the novelty of which will wear off quickly and leave you with exactly the kind of game you didn't think it was?

Probably not. The designers deserve praise for creating a gimmick that really is mechanically integrated with the game. Even if the gimmick gets old, the underlying game design still stays relevant. It's just a great engineering solution (Le Havre and Macao come to mind as examples of games that use similar mechanisms but implemented them differently).

So how about the game? At its core it's the fairly standard "collect resources, convert to victory points" mantra. There is a small tech tree and a (fairly uninspired, I thought) set of buildings and monuments that can be erected for points or other benefits. Despite the tech tree and being able to recruit new workers, Tzolkin is suprisingly light on engine building which is at least part of the reason that the game feels more tactical than strategic despite the planning required to use your workers properly.

This, of course, is where the game differentiates itself from the competition. The restrictions around placing and retrieving your workers, and the increase in power the longer a worker stays put are where the game is. Unlike some other games that have experimented with various twists to the basic worker placement idea, Tzolkin feels fully developed, well-rounded and complete. The cogs are merely a reflection of that thoroughness.

RATING: 6
Played with 'Tribes & Prophecies' expansion

Like most, I appreciate the gears mechanic. I don't think it's gimmicky at all- it solves the problem where all pieces become more valuable after each round, and one doesn't have to worry about any fiddly bit movement, using the gears to shift everything at once. However, once the you get the fundamental system out of the way, you have a very rehashed WP game that offers nothing that hasn't been seen before. And the basic decision of "place worker / remove worker" feels boring after awhile. This game was quite intriguing and even fun to play after the first hour. When we were hitting the 3-hour mark, I felt quite uninspired by the whole thing. The game also has no catch-up mechanisms, which I don't think are always necessary, but new players are usually going to be decimated by seasoned players. There were three new players here. We played around and experimented and two of us were tidily punished by the game for not having a long-range plan to begin with. The third newbie was fortunate enough to pursue a building route, and had enough special powers going into the early next rounds that he could make up for some of his own experimentation.

Anyway, I am a Euro fan and a WP fan. I like the game as it stands as that. But it doesn't do anything intriguing beyond the gears, and those in themselves are nothing but a clean system for shifting pieces. I'd play again, and probably enjoy myself more with the familiarity. But I don't really know if the AP and play time are worth it when there are much cleaner, crisper WP games out there. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Ogrom decyzji w bardzo ograniczonym czasie, ciekawa i oryginalna mechanika. RATING: 2
Half an hour into the game, when I start to feel very bored, someone comes by and says: "Oh, that's the game that made me realize that I don't like euros!".
And even though I am generally a big euro-fan I could understand exactly what he meant.
This was an utterly soulless game machine. And the critique, that with euros you just play against the game rather than against your competitors, never felt so true.
It actually made me quite depressed. Like seeing an illusionist explain and show all his tricks in broad daylight, or realizing that the emperor is naked.
(Or rather: there is nothing wrong with the clothing. The problem is that the emperor is a beutifully dressed up robot.)
Even the cool cogs works as an image of game designing as an engineering process performed by men in white coats in a sterile laboratory, rather than developed round a table in warm and friendly company.
I love a good euro. But this will follow me like a shadow when I see similar mechanics in other games, even if they are used for a better whole. 
No shadow on my fellow gamers at the table though. :)

RATING: 9
Worker placement with an interesting time element.  To perform well, one has to efficiently schedule the placement of his workers and the removal of his workers over a number of turns, while simultaneously competing with other players for scarce board positions.  Seems to have good balance, with room for multiple strategies.  Good bits, although I could do without the glossy board.

On the negative side, Tzolk'in can be something of a trigger for analysis paralysis.  It's also a tricky game to teach, as there are a lot of different elements to go over.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game is great, and honestly, I feel a little bad only rating it an 8, because I think it might be better than that.

It's got exactly what I love in games:  simple, straightforward rules mixed with incredibly deep strategy.  I might be overstating both of those things, but you have two options on your turn:  pick up pawns, or put them down. That's it. And you're only putting them on one of 5 wheels.

Now, WHICH ones do you put them on?  That is the strategy.  Subtle as it is, it's super intricate.  In many ways, it's what I wish Le Havre had going for it - multiple paths to victory.  There really are just a number of ways to win this game I think, and it's going to shift depending on what opponents choose to do, and consequently, leave open.

I think this is a game where tactics come into play a lot.  Strategy is important, but a great strategy is to be adaptable and tactically versatile.

I look forward to playing this game a lot more in the future.

RATING: 8.5

RATING: 7.5
Tzolk'in : The Mayan Calendar is a different kind of worker placement game.  The gears makes this game different and very strategic.
The rules are pretty simple for a game so engaging.  On your turn, you add 1 or more worker OR you remove 1 or more worker.  You do the action when you remove worker.  The longer a worker stays on the board, the better the action.
You really have to plan ahead what you want to do.
From what I've learn in the first game I've played, don't let one player be dominant in all of the three temple!

RATING: 7.5
The calendar gears are the biggest draw to this game, but they are just a fancy and practical way to move pawns in several lines. You perform actions by taking back your pawns, but advancing in those lines/gears gives them more powerful actions. As a result, you will often want to leave pawns on the gears for several turns, and it takes a long time to get things accomplished.

The actions are varied and there are several strategic paths. There is just a little randomness in the starting resources and buildings available, enough to give the game some variety. The design is solid, but it is ultimately an efficiency exercise in gathering resources to improve your engine and convert to victory points - all wrapped in a very thin veil of theme. It takes some time to teach, but the individual actions and bonuses are very simple and intuitive, and the iconography works, so that there aren't many questions during the game.

I know that my description does not sound very exciting, but, surprisingly, I have been enjoying the exploration of the game's possibilities and find myself wanting to play more. I wonder, however, how long that will last.

RATING: 8
Excellent euro brain burn

Updated to a 10 - now about 4/5 games in and it has excellent replayability. Definitely best with 4 and a real brain burner throughout

OK - so I played it a few times more with 2/3... it's actually better with these numbers than I gave it credit for. It's certainly best with 4 but it's better with 2/3 than 95% of games out there (even many that are designed specifically for 2/3)

This game is easily the best game out there and has knocked stone age off it's perch as the number 1 worker placement

---

So my alternative rules which balance it:

Randomise turn order. 1st player takes 3 wealth tiles, 2nd player takes 4, 3rd player takes 5, 4th player takes 6. Any dummy workers get given the standard 6 each for 3-4 player games.

Then starting with the 1st player, select 2 tiles and turn them face up, then 2nd player selects 2, then third selects 2, then 4th.

Advantages: Later players in the turn order get the chance to be more reactive to the expected moves of the earlier players. In the standard rules it is horribly OP in favour of the start player

---

Yeh - I'm downgrading this to 7.5. Don't get me wrong, I still like it and the mechanisms in it are very clever and more than just a gimmick. But the lack of balance and quality of the dominant strategy mean it can become a bit dull

RATING: 8
It seems like just another worker placement game--place workers to gather resources, which are then turned into buildings and VP. However, with asymmetric starting conditions for each player (like in Leonardo Da Vinci), and five circular queues controlled by a master calendar, this is one insanely intriguing and fascinating design.

The circular queue is arguably the most unique game innovation to date, giving a breath of fresh air to what has become a glut of "me too" worker placement games. I was beginning to tire of the next boring, soulless iteration in this genre, but this has got me thinking and excited again about worker placement games. Each queue can be "programmed" with one or more workers, depending on how far up each spot is. With each game turn, the master calendar turns each queue up one notch, with progressively better and more powerful choices. However, a player can only place or remove workers, but not both, making for delicious, agonizing decisions with every click of the gears.

While there are some random and hidden elements (i.e., starting resources/positions, buildings, monuments), it is near-perfect information, much like Puerto Rico. The decision space is very large and seems overwhelming at first, but becomes manageable, as killer combinations begin to materialize. The decision tree is constantly changing with every player's worker placement and removal; with three other players sharing the queues, it is rather chaotic like Roborally. Still, long term planning becomes key, say two to three turns ahead.

Replayability is high, since starting conditions for each player is different for every game. With the variety of buildings and monuments available also being different every game, this effectively nulls out any standard "opening move". These random elements also set the tone and theme for each game, keeping it fresh and new.

Components are top-notch, typical of Rio Grande. Rules are quite dense, but well written. However, a number of the finer points of the rules were missed or misinterpreted. After several games, most of the game concepts become intuitive, and the play goes quicker and smoother. The initial two plays clocked in at 2.5 hours for 4-players, but I suspect this could get down to the published 90 minute figure with more experience. Still, there may be occasional "bumps" of AP in the first plays.

This is an exceptional game, in terms of gaming innovation, novelty, and simple fun. The chopping of trees and harvesting of corn is reminiscent of Antiquity, without the pollution. The action spaces are quite varied and interesting, not like in a majority of other worker placement games. There is the feeling of desperation, as the calendar ticks away, much like The Princes of Machu Picchu. The queues remind me of the varied production calendars in Eve:Conquests. The overall look and feel is along the lines of Hawaii. This game does a wonderful job of invoking the feel of many of these games, and yet it is a unique animal, all with a feel of its own. The fact that it has risen so quickly through the BGG rankings is a testament to its novelty and thoughtfully engaging gameplay. It's a solid '8'.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
Too much of the game scoring is dependant on the religion track. You must pray adequately to Mayan deities or be elbowed for your lack of faith at every feeding round with a final knee to a groin at end-game scoring. Any player who attempted a strategy devoid of faith will be knocked out by the Mayan gods fairly early-on.

Thus match-ups are pretty samey in each game. Either copiously pray and try do so economically or pay the price for being an atheist. The score differential is massive between a devout Mayan shaman versus a sacrilegious heathen. No religion at your own peril.

RATING: 9.6
Best Game of the Year!! Well 2013 just started but this game was technically released in Europe in 2012 so let me clarify by saying Best Game of 2012!! Wow, I have not been this excited since I played Power Grid in 2010. What makes this game Great:
1) Fancy artwork and visually appealing once the cogs are painted. 
2) Not just your regular worker placement game but with a new time mechanism by rotating gears which syncs with other gears. Turning the cogs represent time/days passing and coordinating your workers to combo actions is a great woohoo(Simpsons) feeling. It is like party planning where you send out multiple people to different locations to gather food, drinks, supplies, decorations, entertainment and having them all return at the exact time.
3) Multiple paths to victory. There are more than one way to score points (either temples, skulls, monuments or buildings) and different approaches to them.
4) Random starting tiles and monuments will alter your winning strategies from the last.
5) Low Luck. A new player is very unlikely to beat an experience player. 
6) Great with 2-4 players.
7) The game play is elegant and smooth. You are mainly placing or removing workers. 

Go get it !

RATING: 6

RATING: 8
Oooh, this is cool.  The gears look gimmicky, and I guess it would be possible to have the game without them if you didn't mind making the game RIDICULOUSLY FIDDLY, but not only do the gears save you from that, but also, come one, this board is awesome. The game is pretty cool too. As one might expect, it's a planning game, and a pretty intense one. However, it doesn't feel as stressful to the new player as planning-heavy games often can; it's important to get the people in the right place at the right time, but plans can change, and it's all kind of a balancing act trying to manage it correctly.  That's not the whole game, either; there's a tradeoff between resources and points, there's jockeying for position on the god tracks, and especially, there are the special ability tiles which make a huge difference in the game.  This is really cool, you guys. (4 plays)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Tzolk'in, The Mayan Calendar is a worker placement game where you place your pieces on one of 5 gears that are all controlled by a large central gear. On your turn you either place one or more workers OR remove one or more workers. When you remove a worker, you take the action. Every turn the gear moves, improving the actions for all of the placed workers. So this is a game where timing your actions on multiple gears to get and do what you need to is key. There are a lot of ways to score points and I think we learned that you best figure out what to specialize in early and go for it.  

An attractive and fresh-feeling take on a mechanic (worker placement) that is seeing a fair amount of over-use the last few years.

RATING: 5

Sorry to say, I have to post a somewhat negative commentary on this game.  The spinning wheels look great, and everyone was initially excited about the game.  Now only a few die-hards will play it in tournaments.  I got in around a dozen plays and after the first three or four, I got less and less interested in playing it.  Why?  Big problem is lack of multiple paths to victory.  To me, the key is moving up the temple track as it scores four times during the game (twice in bonus resources, twice in bonus points).  Then the game devolves into devising the best tactics to accomplish this, which to me is not very interesting or thematic.

RATING: 8.5
Extreme Worker Placement.   

Man, this is a stressful game!  It seems like your back is up against the wall every single turn, while you manage what little resources and actions you have in order to scrounge together a few points here and there.  Perhaps this is due to unfamiliarity still, having only played twice, but it sure seems like there's is a plethora of things to do and only a fraction of the time needed to do them in.  The wheel spins slowly enough (one notch per round), and yet before you know it, you're frantically trying to make sure your workers can be fed, or attempting to get some influence in a temple before the scoring round begins!   

More plays are needed to fairly and more accurately assess this unique and interesting (but tiring and Brain burning) title.  For now, based on production values and originality, I give it a 7.5.     

EDIT:  After more than 5 plays over the past few months, with 2, 3, and 4 players, I'd say this is just above an 8 rating for me.  Simply put, it's gone from being just a good game, to bordering on great.  Perhaps staying power will push it into the 8.5 or even 9 rating.  For now, I'll settle at 8.25.

RATING: 9
Excellent euro with a truly unique mechanic. Before getting to that there are many things that are not new, resource collection, multiple paths to VP, and worker placement. The game is surprisingly simple to explain, the rules are not complex however the game play is full of calculation and hard decisions centred around which actions are best to select and how best to plan your moves.

The unique mechanic is the gear which pushes workers placed to new actions each round. Your turn is very simple, either place workers or remove them and perform the action they are standing at. The gears move each round which moves your workers to different actions. Each of the 5 gears has a different class of action to perform, e.g. collect resources, collect money (corn), build/technology. 

There is player interaction in several places. Of course worker placement but also the game is a bit contrarian as there are three potentially large sources of points (temples, skulls, monuments) and paying close attention to the actions of the others is important.

The game has great tension from lack of actions and time to do everything. You really have to make sure you're ambitions are realistic. We've found that the game actually plays in about 90min as written on the box making this pretty fast and very enjoyable. The fun/time ratio is much higher than expected.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Love the graphics and the gears... great tension and planning... good all around.

RATING: 6
There's a lot going on in the Mayan Calendar. Be prepared to spend a few hours with it when you have AP prone comrades around. At least, you're spending these hours for a game that manages to do something new in boardgaming with the much discussed gears. What they manage to do is introducing duration as a strategic dimension of playing. The longer you stay with an action the better it gets. This stems from the possibility to either place workers or remove them in your turn. A decision which has been featured in a few worker placement games before but the first time as prominent as here. Secondary to this, there are multiple dimensions fueled by the gears. Money represented by corn has to be managed, an engine building part is plugged on, there's a race for the highest position on three pyramids and various victory point or ability granting buildings can be bought. Enough opportunities for gamers to indulge into the depth generated by the hundred small parts. It's a competently developed and finetuned game which I enjoyed but don't love. The depth is too much complexity driven for my liking and the necessary invest is high for the enjoyment payout provided. It's one of the games where I see myself struggling whenever I play this and never coming to a satisfactory level of play. It's in good company with Stone Age, Goa and Puerto Rico.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
The new mechanic here is the aging of your meeples to get better actions.  Sort of like the Ora & Labora aging except that the result is more versatile.  The gears do a good job of implementing this mechanic without too much overhead.

Complementing this new mechanic is several technology tracks, several victory point tracks, buildings, and end game monuments.

The new mechanic is interesting.  The question is if the rest of the complementary parts are well put together for a lasting and replayable game.

RATING: 6
There's no shortage of games where you figuratively spin the gears of a resource and action procuration layer, investing the proceeds in efficiency increases, one-shot bonuses and, sooner or later, victory points. Tzolk'in distinguishes itself by providing actual wheels to spin your pieces into a resource and action procuration timescape, although the investment part seems to offer little new. I like it better than, say, Stone Age, but more play is needed to deterine how much. "Count backwards from the end" syndrome features on long and short time scales, alleviated by a bit of multiplayer chaos.

RATING: 8.5
A sprinkling of Stone Age and Ora et Labora, with a surprisingly gentle learning curve. The group of three strangers whom I played this with and I picked it up after a fifteen-minute rules summary without a problem, even though it looks daunting at first. I can see why this was one of the most touted games at BGG.con - it's really engaging and tricky, with some of the family-feeding stress of Agricola with a slightly easier target, and the strategies are varied as the starting materials (which can give you more actions, more stuff, more abilities, all while maintaining a pretty astounding level of balance). 

I'll definitely buy this when I get a chance. It's almost made for a computer emulation on boiteajeux or boardgamearena. I'm a pretty big fan and I'm looking forward to giving it another shot.

RATING: 9
Hogy milyen a játék? Szerintem briliáns! Brutálisan sok lehetőség van benne. Az alapmechanizmus szinte pofátlanul egyszerű. Vagy el kell helyezned egy (vagy több) munkást valamelyik fogaskeréken, vagy fel kell venned őket. Ennyi. 
Hogy közben élelmiszert takarítasz be, nyersanyagot termelsz ki, kereskedsz, építkezel, technológiát fejlesztesz, gyarapítod a munkásaid számát, kristálykoponyákra hajtasz, vagy esetleg keresed az istenek jóindulatát, az már csak rajtad múlik.
Ami a legszebb az egészben, hogy nincs egyértelműen legjobb taktika. Bár valószínűleg véges a győzelemhez vezető optimális kombinációk száma, azért nem csekély lehetőségről van szó. Bár nincsenek direkt konfliktusok a játékosok között, azért egymás bábui befolyásolják a stratégiák alakulását.
A játék 12-es karikás. Én lehet, hogy még magasabbra raknám, vagy inkább úgy fogalmaznám meg, hogy nem árt némi társasjátékos előképzettség hozzá. Elmélyülésre képes középiskolásoknak sima ügy. Persze egyénfüggő, biztosan lenne olyan gyerek, aki 10 évesen is tudná élvezni. Azért alapvetően inkább felnőtt játéknak mondanám. És bár nem állítom, hogy ezen az oldalon (BGG) elért pontszámok, értékelések szentírások, de ha egy játék kb. 2-3 évvel a megjelenése után a több tízezer játék közül a 16. helyen áll úgy, hogy ebben a pillanatban 10198 szavazat alapján 8.00/10 a pontszáma, azért az mégiscsak jelent valamit... Nem?

RATING: 5
Another no-luck, full information worker placement game (well, technically some building cards are added during the game). On the plus side Tzolk'in looks great and has multiple paths to victory and so on. On the negative side the game is long, static, surprisingly forgiving (there are several rules to make sure you can't get hosed or have trouble timing how the wheels move) and it doesn't really develope during its lengthy playing time so you'll feel like you're doing the same thing over and over again. The gimmick (wheels) doesn't really add anything, altough it does make the game tiny bit less fiddly as you don't need to manually move each worker along a path as you do in other similar games.

RATING: 6
The heart of this game is a retread. Get stuff and cash it in for points, choosing to specialize in this or that. 

The end-game scoring tiles seem like they could make the game better, by presenting different areas of specialization that are clearly better than others but I don't see the conflict that comes from that making an entire game interesting. 

The gears are neat but unessential. The timing decisions they introduce are sometimes interesting but all in all I think for a game that packs about as much punch as something like Stone Age you've got alot of rules investment for a lean payoff, relative to other games. A solid 7 "JASE" masquerading as something more than it is, and that added complexity makes me lower the rating to a 6. A game I'll play without protest at least a few more times but I'd be trading it if I bought it.

RATING: 6.5
Damn, I'm sure it's a well-developed game but it was such a letdown...

However cool it looks, it's a very typical excel sheet game, manipulating columns with values until you get the most points possible.

As for the wheels: as a mechanism, they are columns made more usable. They are simply spectacular forms of columns (the game would also work if you pushed each worker down by one in a column at the end of each turn). So I don't call this a very interesting mechanism, even though it's more usable than columns and looks cool.

So what is the interesting part of the mechanism? To me, it's that you EITHER put some workers there OR you take your workers off (and do the actions). This is interesting. But that's it.

The rest is something you can expect from an Agricola- and Caylus-loving designer. Nothing really new: familiar ideas (favor track from here, feeding your workers from there, building boring buildings, going up some tracks that score you points and give some in-game benefits), multiple ways to win, also I'm sure it's well-balanced, only... I just felt I don't really care.
Besides the lack of interesting ideas, what I really missed was some sense of theme here. You get different looking markers but this game is not more thematic, than, say, Keltis. Agricola has lots of theme. Caylus has theme and cool mechanism ideas. Goa has a great central mechanism. Even Shipyard has a lot more theme and moving your ships on the river. For this complexity a game needs some more theme to remain interesting and entertaining.

The visual gimmick is nice, the rest is just not outstanding for me in any way. Probably it's only because it's not really my favorite subgenre (although I love all the games mentioned above), but I expected more.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
There's a lot to like here. Aesthetically, it's just a neat looking game, with the crazy cool gears in the middle. The gears provide a neat twist on worker placement, where you can let a worker ride around and take more valuable actions (also I like how you can place or remove workers at will, instead of the usual all-or-nothing in WP.) It's also a plus that it has a moderately novel theme (honestly, Mayans and Egyptians and Polynesians and such can all be lumped into the same "pagan barbarians" category) that the gear attempts to work with (as sick as I am of steampunk and such, I see those big gears and think Fritz Lang's Metropolis and wouldn't that have been kind of cool!) 

With so much to like, naturally it doesn't really excite me that much. It reminds me a lot of Trajan, in that if you step away from the novel mechanic, you're left with the same old tired Eurogame junk. Get resources. Advance on this track. Build buildings. Feed workers. It doesn't feel particularly Mayan or anything, really, just more of the same. So I'm left rating a single mechanism, not a game. As a mechanism the gears are neat. The approach to worker placement is really nice, and a promising development. Figuring out how to play the gears is interesting, to a point (far more interesting than fiddling with that Trajan mancala.) I just wish the freshness extended beyond the gears, because the mundanity of everything else about Tzolkin nearly overpowers the novelty.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I do not care much for worker placement games.  As one critic once said, "They're soulless."  There's a common formula to worker placement games, then just slap a theme on there and make a few adjustments to the formula so it fits your needs and originality.  Tzolk'in takes everything I just said and throws it out the window.  The only reason I don't give this a 10, is because I'm not a fan of worker placement games.  For starters, this game is beautiful.  It has a gorgeous board that is lush with color.  The artwork does a good job of tying everything together.  

There were four of us playing and I came in dead last.  It was my first time playing so there's a lot to wrap your head around, but this game is just so incredibly well thought out.  I have to laugh when someone says the gears on the game board are a gimmick.  No, that's a very unique, functional mechanic of the game.  Rest assured, it's no gimmick.  This game requires foresight and plenty of silent mental planning that can easily be thwarted when a gear your worker is on suddenly moves farther than you considered.  

These ideas of burning down a forest to get to the corn as well as angering the gods in the process is far beyond clever, and that's just one of several aspects in this game.  Constructing buildings and monuments, moving up the temple tracks and collecting (and utilizing) crystal skulls round out some of the other major things to do in the game.  I have no idea how someone sat down and said "I want to create this Mayan-themed game that does this, this and this," but they did, and did so beautifully.

RATING: 7

Neat twist on the worker placement games as the longer you keep a worker in place the better the results will be. You still have to take care as to your timing because you can't take a workers actions and place workers in the same turn so you might miss a specific action you want to take and have to spend a little extra food or time to get it.

There are several different things to put your mind to in this one as well. I'm not sure if one route or the other is better or not though but time will tell.

RATING: 8.5
The gears are not just a novelty; although their effect could have been emulated with just a regular game board, this would have been so fiddly that people would have dismissed the game.

This is a genuine innovation in the worker-placement genre, and it lacks the feeling of sameness that plagues games such as Egizia.

Timing is important, more-so than in other games in the genre. The fact that you have to choose each turn between placing workers, or getting their benefit, means that you have to consider carefully how often you do either.

RATING: 8
Worker Placement with a twist - 5 cogs (wheels) connected to a central cog (wheel) - Place your workers on one or more of the small cogs and at the end of your turn the big cog moves which of course moves all the little ones - on your turn you MUST either be placing a cog/cogs or removing a cog/cogs - the long you ride the cog the better the resources / abilities you get.
Many routes to scoring points
great thinky game

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Cool looking material, original wheel mechanism for "timed" worker placement and well constructed game mechanics.  

Long down-time? Definitely! Do you need more than 2 plays to understand how to get control over this game, yes you do (at least I do).

In the beginning, this game is pure frustration. Running behind the facts, concentrating on food to feed your workers, and making sure you put your workers to work soon enough, so you can harvest from your investment on the longer term.

The moving wheels mechanism makes me think of [thing=55670][/thing]. But this game is not about cards. Timing is everything, and also, you need to make sure that you don't get lost in all the options you have. In that sense this game belongs to the same category as [thing=104006][/thing], but then [thing=104006][/thing] can be seen as the lighter version of Tzolk'in.

So is this a game you should play? Sure! If all the players know the game and know what they are doing, this could be a fun game. But until you all get there ... 

Then the question is, is it all worth the investment (2-3 hours play) ? The game is certainly not boring. There is a lot going on and a lot to think about. The best planner will definitely win this game. And if not, it will be because of the buildings.

Having said all that, once you have played this game a few times, you start wondering how you could do better. So let me conclude this game's fun, lies in the sleepless nights you have after a few defeats, thinking which extreme strategy is going to bring you victory next time.

And if you don't mind cheating, you can read about the best strategies on BGG. But then it becomes more of a study of the best opening move and stuff like that. And that is where, I give up. When a game needs study to play it well, it's no longer a game, it's an exam.

Minus: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
This is not the typical worker-placement game. This is not the typical cube-pushing game. This is not the typical VPs-salad game or VP-engine game - in fact very few actions grant VPs. 

Tzolk'in is a very solid, thoughtful and deep design. The gears mechanic is more than just a gimmick; it's very essential to the game. It represents the timely placement of your workers in order to be effective. Because of that and the absence of virtually any luck, the best and more experienced player will always win. I don't think that the learning curve is very steep, but you need a few plays to really appreciate the depth of the actions in order to use them in your favor (i.e. in a coherent way). 

Arguably, Tzolk'in is one of the best worker-placement games out there. If you like this mechanic, then you must own this game. But then, "why the low rating?" you might ask. Because, although I can appreciate its design, it just isn't my type of game. It's not fun. It feels more like an exercise. I also have to add that the end-game is quite anti-climactic. In all our games it was obvious who would win at least 3-4 rounds before the end.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Okay - let me state straight away that I think Tzolk’in is, in all probability, an excellent game. I love worker placement; I love bits that interact; I love difficult decisions in games… I had, to a point, a really good time playing it. … However, I have issues with the game, most of which are not to do with the game itself, but more to do with how I operate.

 Tzolk’in, if you haven’t heard of it, is a game based on quite thorough research of the Maya Calendar, its Long Cycle, Short Cycle and the various periods which make up those (I’d be interested to know if the different cogs have numbers of teeth which equate to the various short periods which make up the numerous elements, but as I couldn’t remember what those period numbers were, I couldn’t check). 

In essence, it’s a worker placement game (yay!, my favourite (not joking)), but with a twist: the main mechanism consists of a series of interlocking cogs which are an active part of the board. By active, I mean that the board itself affects your decision making, and it will change, not necessarily to your liking, dependent on player actions. For the most part, you could regard the board action as predictable, but you can’t guarantee it.

 On their turn, players have one choice: place workers and pay the cost for placement; or remove workers and receive the benefit which results from their board positions. Players may not combine placement and removal - it is either/or, in a similar fashion to The Manhattan Project. The number of actions is limited by how many workers the player chooses to place or remove and, in the case of placement, whether the player has enough food to pay for the placement.

 Once each player has taken their actions the turn has one last phase, which is for the interlocking wheels to rotate, thus moving all the workers to new spots where you may or may not like the benefits. 

Now, I don’t want to spend too much time describing how the game works because there’s far too much going on in this game (FAR too much) for me to describe it adequately in less time than the game plays. (Maybe you can do that, but I can’t, or am not willing to. If you know me, you know what I mean and shall be thankful; or if you’re just browsing this, then you have to take it as read that, really, this is the easier option and you can all thank whatever god you pray to, or just shrug your shoulders and live-and-let-live.) Instead, try to imagine someone making a design decision to combine Caylus with Mousetrap (slightly facetious of me) and a funky mechanical version of the card-shifting function in Through the Ages… but you only get cards for which you have placed a worker on a previous turn…

 Yes, it sounds shit when I explain it like that, but then I try not to compromise when it comes to quality.

 So, here we are, with the genius of the game: the rotating actions/costs/benefits; which also happen to be, in my case, its downfall… or should I say, in everyone else’s case, should I happen to be one of the players. The problem is this: I have a problem. I am not a linear thinker. I do not absorb rules by reading the rule book and knowing what happens. I absorb rules by practical experience, by iteration and re-iteration, and I communicate my experience in the same fashion. I think this is a function of my employment - for more than twenty years, I have worked in an industry which demands prioritisation: thinking is cyclical and rewards an approach which discards everything but the most important and immediate event and its effect, then, once that event and effect is processed and accommodated, one can proceed to re-process the list of required events, select the most important, discard the others and work on the event required. This makes me quite successful in some game genres, especially those which are tactical rather than strategic.

The trouble with Tzolki’in is it requires assessment of the current, future and far future possibilities simultaneously, and my brain just doesn’t work like that. The result is that I suffer from an embarrassing - and almost debilitating - level of analysis paralysis.

Now, AP is not something that I find predictable. In many games, I don’t feel I suffer at all (though my wife would disagree); but there are some in which I brain freeze like I’ve swallowed a pint of Cherry Garcia. Of all the games I’ve encountered, Tzolk’in is the Primate (in the sense of first, rather than ape-like). And this is my problem with the game: if I’d been playing against myself (not solo, because solo games bore the hell out of me - I mean, who wants to play games which don’t involve other people - surely that’s the point?), I wouldn’t have felt so self-conscious. But from the first turn, I knew I would be the game’s problem and started apologising. This is a good game (gimmicky, yes, but still a good - and perhaps even great - game). I liked what it did. I REALLY liked how it did it; I loved the fact that the game principles can be communicated in a sentence, though it needs an in-depth explanation to give details of what that place-workers-or-remove-workers mechanism actually entails; I was excited and entertained by the novelty of worker movement.

What broke the game for me was ME: because I was so consciously aware of the time I was taking, as the game progressed I felt I was getting wearing for everyone else. Whether this was true or not is irrelevant (it’s true, by the way): it’s how I felt. This meant enjoyment for me decreased as the game progressed, and my ability to apply my analysis to the game diminished as my worry about how I was impacting others outside the gamespace began to crowd into my play.

 I won’t bore anyone else with further details, but suffice to say that this is the best and most interesting game I’ve played and which I’m unlikely to play again. I’m just too boring when I get like this. It’s no fun for anybody else, and it’s no fun for me when I know I”m affecting the enjoyment of others.

Seldom have I played a game when I’ve finished feeling so torn; and never have I played a game and finished feeling so psychologically aware of my own failings.

Make no mistake: this is a game I feel warrants extended play, and which I’m itching to play again, but I feel my inability to process its information quickly demands that I step away.

 I am very disappointed.

RATING: 9
We have read the instructions and set up the gaming board.  The design looks solid and the gears look practical in addition to their bell and whistleness.  My wife loves the theme and has been to most of the ruins.  Now lets find out if it is any fun to play!

We both really enjoyed our first game.  The game moves along a lot quicker than I thought it would and I realized I had to be more judicious about what I hoped to accomplish.  I managed to succeed at my paired back monument plan in the last turn and get a large bonus.  The monuments remind me of the Big Buildings in Puerto Rico, but I like that they will be variable from game to game.  It seems like it will be important to choose an overall strategy each game that is in alignment with the available monuments.

Now lets see how the replayability of this game holds up.  Definitely looking forward to the next game.

I should also mention that this game is lighter than a lot of our other Euros.  My wife even proclaimed with bluster that this is a game we could play on a work night, but this is only in comparison to say Mage Knight or such.  I think calling it a work night game is a bit of an exaggeration, but this does mean that we can hopefully teach this to some of our friends/family who like games but are not as hard core as the two of us.

Update 1
We are playing our second game and there is really great strategy here.  The game is very elegant and even deeper than I initially thought.  Moved up to a 9.

Very easy to set up and the game runs itself so you can focus on playing.  Of course this only matters if playing is fun, which it is!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
The gears are really cool. You place workers onto them. On a later turn you can remove workers from gears to receive resources or actions and the longer you've waited, the more the gears have turned increasing your awards. As great as that is I was a bit put off by how calculating the game is. You see, timing on the gears is crucial and each turn you must place or remove workers. So if you have no workers to place then you must remove at least one from the gears, perhaps not having received the resources you had hoped for. It might just take a couple of games to best understand the rhythm of the game.

My rating has gone up 1/2 a point after playing it 3-player. Seems to be the sweet spot as you can plan much better. You have a much better understanding of what the board/gears will look like when it comes back to your turn. Already in two games I've seen multiple paths to victory, although it's clear that the moving up on the temples is very important.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.9
This game only made it to table once thus far, but what a game it is.  Big honking plastic cogs make up the center of the board and thus the mechanics are: the central calendar gear moves all the different-sized gears at different rates, each of which holds your worker placement and moves them around in sequence.  Your primary concern, after placement, is figuring out just what point to take the worker off - and the place they get removed determines what benefits you get. 

It has been a long while but the game took some getting used to, because it goes along the path towards the forest that IS Victory Point Salad - lots of weird crap to get used to, and try to prioritize, all while getting used to the delightful mechanical mechanics of the game.

A game I look forward to bringing to table again some time with the right group of people.

(Written 4/27/16)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A typical, by-the-books euro (get resources/build buildings/get VP on different tracks) dressed up with a very cool gimmick: the gears.

This is a game of timing and managing your resources (aren't they all?). Each turn consists of placing workers onto the board or taking them off of the board, that's it. The catch is that at the end of each round, all of the gears rotate 1 space, improving the rewards for removing the workers there. So it's a matter of jockeying for position and making sure you have the food to be able to place your workers and remove them at the right time. 

So the gears are eye-catching and have a definite toy factor to them, but they are also very, very interesting and central to the gameplay.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
A fogaskerekes munkáslehelyezés és elvétel tényleg nagyon ötletes! Nem hasonlítható más általam játszott játékokhoz. Különleges megközelítést kíván, tényleg nem tudom eléggé dicsérni. Nagyon sok lehetőség közül lehet választani, és sok minden összefügg a táblán, ezért egy ügyes játékos remek kombókat alkothat, ami mindig kitűnő érzés.
Viszont gyakorlatilag az elején eldől minden. Ha valaki jól kezd, akkor könnyen behozhatatlan előnyre tehet szert, és a játék hosszát tekintve (2,5-3 óra négyen) ez szenvedés a többieknek. Engem nem is az zavar, hogy tudom, hogy nem nyerek, mert maga a fogaskerekes mechanizmus és a kombózás szórakoztat (hiszen nem a győzelem az elsődleges cél, hanem a szórakozás). De nagyon minimális az interakció, nem igazán lehet keresztbe tenni az ellenfelednek. Ebből következik, hogy a nyerésre álló játékost, nem lehet akadályozni, illetve, hogy óriási a holtidő a játékban. Gyakorlatilag érdektelen számomra, hogy mit csinál a másik, aki ráadásul, ha ügyesen feltápolta magát, hosszú óriáskombókba kezdhet, amit mind "magában pakolgat". Ez nem túl izgalmas.
Ja és még valami (bár ez legutóbb az én problémám volt..) Ha pont egy tapasztalt játékos után ül az ember (aki elég jól áll), nehéz helyzetbe tud kerülni: minden drágává válik, vagy kezdőjátékos leszel és akkor a 0-sra kell pakolni..
Sajnálom a dolgot, mert a mechanika remek, amit a saját körömben élvezek, viszont a hibák miatt, amikor nem én vagyok, rém unalmas; ebből pedig körönként 3 van.. Az interakció hiánya és a játék hossza sajnos eltántorít a további Tzolkinozástól.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A meatier Euro than it first appears, Tzolk'in makes use of gears that adds a real twist to the worker placement genre because the players not only have to place their workers, they know have to try and work out where they will end up in certain turns to trigger the effect they want to access. 

Problem with gears is that when one gear moves thanks to another player, it will move the other gears too and that can foul up your plans. 

This is slick design and well worth a look but it can be a little more mind bending than some Euro fans prefer. That was certainly the case for me but the innovative design also makes it a title that many a game collector will want to have in their collection.

RATING: 9
While some aspects about the game feel familiar, the gears add something completely new and unique. It's not just gimmick, but adds a new style of gameplay. The rules and mechanics are simple to teach and learn, but chaining together meaningful moves takes practice and careful planning. Unlike most worker placement games, performing one needed action may take several turns. This makes the game brilliant. I also enjoy the balance and multiple paths to victory. The theme and components are great and add to this  overall amazing game.

RATING: 8
Perhaps my favorite worker placement game to date. There are so many paths to victory, and even though I suck at the game, I enjoy it every time I play. It's a game where the rules are fairly straight-forward, but the decision space is vast. I've heard that there are broken strategies, but honestly this seems to be mostly group-dependent. Usually whoever is the odd person out, meaning that they go for a different strategy than everyone else, tends to end up winning. This can be a plus or minus depending on who you are. The gears are a neat novelty, but it's really the brain-burning decisions that make Tzolk'in so great.

RATING: 5
Tzolk'in is most known for its innovative gears that automatically increase the workers' return the longer they stay on a gear. It's an elegant solution that poses to the players the challenge of return the workers early with a lower return or more often with a higher return. The return also requires a payment of corn and/or resources, something that you frequently find yourself lacking.

However, keeping track of all bonuses is more fiddly and it's not always obvious what the iconography means. In addition, it gets a bit repetitive after a while to send out and return workers with little sense of progress.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Players are Mayan tribes, placing workers on turning gears. Each round the gears rotate, and players must either remove workers to take their action, or place workers on gears. The further along the gear, the more valuable the action.

Each player is seeded with 4 wealth tiles, and you choose two of them. During the game, you need corn to feed workers, stone/wood/gold for buildings and monuments, and skulls for VPs. There are four different technology tracks to advance in, which give you benefits during the game. One also has to worry about being higher on a temple to ean VPs, so there is a lot going on here.

This game was one of the highest regarded at Essen 2012. I'm going to really enjoy discovering the many layers this game has to offer and I really look forward to more plays. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
More than just a gimmick. A bloomin' hard to manage resource game! Food is a constant struggle and one of the few worker placement games where for most of the game you want LESS workers not more!

RATING: 9
This game kicks my ass and I love it. Often find myself in complete failure, almost starving, and certainly never managing to get anything I wanted. Brilliant.

Although, to be honest, I wonder if the Monuments are a but underpowered. Every time I go for them, it ends in disaster. I think they are suppossed to be more sort of a 'sub plot' rather than the main goal, but they look and feel like they should be more important than they are. And the god track is a hell lot more important than it looks and feels too.

Some people I guess are just as happy that way, but it's a knock against the game for me. I mean: what's more of a fun goal: building buildings, or moving up a track??

I wonder to what degree all this is player dependent though. I mostly play 2 player of this game, and that makes the God tracks more powerful.

Still, cool game. I love being on the edge of disaster and just scraping out success (or at least survival) :)

*Also own: Tribes and Prophecies*

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Beautiful and original game. I don't agree with the criticisms I have read here on BGG, i.e. the game being yet-another worker placement game, with a new mechanic. The gears and the whole timing strategy ARE original and not only on the mechanical level, the entire game is different because of them.

Even without considering its "originality" factor, the game manages to integrate already seen mechanics in a beautiful whole.

The map and the components, and the way the gears are used, increase the WOW factor: this game is somehow an event in the hobby.

It is highly replayable. There are many paths to victory, and I like that one can try out different strategies every time one plays, and see how they work, as funny experiments.

It scales well (in 3 and 4 players at least, I have not tried the game in two, but I guess that it is also good in two).

The only reason it does not get a "10" from me is that I miss a bit the strategic depth and tension that I appreciate in my preferred games (e.g. Ora et Labora). Sometimes it seems that the small tactical moves and calculations have a bigger impact than I would like for the "perfect" strategy game.

In any case this game should be present in any collection, also because it is somehow a middle-weight game (possibly as middle weight game my preferred one) and so approachable to less "hard" gamers, with a short playing time (another plus, it seems that so much happens in approx. 90 minutes). So very easy to find occasions to play it.

Recommended.

RATING: 9
Gimmick or not?

I say no.  It may have started out as a gimmick, it may have been the reason I bought it, but the game play behind the gimmick is solid enough that you will not hear me complaining.  

Place your workers on cogs so that turns later you can remove them to get bonuses and rewards.  Usually the longer you keep a worker on a cog the bigger and better the advantage will be when removing said worker.

Manage your buildings, temples, god favors, crystal skulls, and a few other items to win this gem of a game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Super difficult for the brain to process on the first play; reminds me of my first game of Agricola: so much to do, time flies, and suddenly you can't do a single thing at endgame. Like Caylus, it's important to amass gold to build the prestige buildings. Like Agricola, it's important to have your meeple feeding squared away and to acquire more meeple. Like Macao/Trajan/Ora & Labora, you have to plan your moves in advance so as to minimize doing just one thing on a turn. Like Le Havre, you plan your moves and then too late realize that you're short a resource on the turn when you were going to do that thing. With a combo of elements from such highly-rated games, no wonder this game has shot up the rankings without warning to land at #17 as I write this. A real brain burner, this one, but it might get much, much easier on subsequent plays, like Agricola did. For now I'm marking it as Heavy, even though it plays quickly for a Heavy game--the litmus test being that afterward, everyone just wanted to go home, and it was only 10:30.
------
Second play was indeed much easier, but I still couldn't build the monument I wanted due to again mis-assessing the point in the game in which it was necessary to get my ducks lined up. It seemed like there was more time than there really was (thus is the calendar theme freighted with even greater meaning).

[color=#117bc0][b]Probably rather play:[/b][/color] Amyitis

RATING: 8
The highlight of the game is the gears. Not only an inspired way of speeding up the upkeep between rounds, they also allow the progression of time to become an easily trackable and usable factor with the actions of workers getting better as they age. I also like that having more workers isn't an obviously good thing - becoming prohibitively expensive to use the full complement. The rest of the game is solid, though familiar, worker placement fare, with feeding workers, progressing along tracks and building engines to do actions more efficiently.

I feel slightly disappointed that every time the gears are turned a random worker doesn't disappear to his doom through a hole in the board.

Je m’intéresse de plus en plus à ce style de jeu, surtout la partie placement d'ouvrier qui est au cœur de ce jeu.
Ce jeu pourrait être qualifié comme étant familial ++ mais je préfère le qualifier de kubenbois accessible.
Déjà la thématique claque pour un jeu kubenbois : l'univers Maya est bien retranscrit et cohérent même si ce n'est pas le plus important dans ce jeu, ça fait quand même plaisir car c'est un minimum immergeant.
Le gros point positif de ce jeu ce sont les roues du calendrier Maya. Cette mécanique de roue ingénieusement associée au placement d'ouvrier font que l'on va pouvoir calculer pas mal de coup fumants plusieurs tours à l'avance et quelle satisfaction cela apporte de les voir se réaliser :-).
La richesse du jeu est également un bon point positif : il y a énormément d'actions possibles, de combinaisons, de stratégie à mettre en place. Les batiments et monuments à construire apportent également leur lot de diversification pour améliorer le potentiel de rejouabilité.
Côté matériel, c'est également un point positif car le plateau, les roues, les tuiles et les kubenbois sont de qualités. Par contre comme bien souvent hélas : pas de rangement adapté dans la boite :-( tous les kubenbois et tuiles sont dans des sachets plastiques tout mélangés. Si on prévoit pas mal de partie, une boite de rangement me parait indispensable.
Pour moi le seul point négatif réside dans les règles, surtout la quantité de règles et comment se les approprier. Le livret est très clair et le web ne manque pas de vidéos explicatives. Cependant, il est très difficile voir impossible de commencer la partie sans avoir fait le tour complet de toutes les actions du jeu sur le plateau. Même si on se permet une première partie un peu free style, cette phase d'apprentissage des règles est un peu douloureuse ... Une fois mise en pratique tout devient clair et intuitif cependant. C'est un peu comme la découverte d'un bon fromage de chèvre : au début ça pique, si on fait pas attention on vomit partout, puis avec de la pratique on a toujours envie d'explorer tellement c'est bon et ça un gout de reviens-y.
Pour moi un très bon jeu stratégique/calculatoire qui procure des sensations bien sympa quand on voit se réaliser nos plans sur plusieurs tours.

通常是前期累積資源 升科技
後期爆發的遊戲
評價第二場後上升了
規則:
1.照人數放玉米田，起始占人，紀念碑版塊，綠色指標指向頭
2.起始三人，資源版塊四選二

4.少於兩玉米，可以降神廟一格補到三玉米
5.可付差額玉米做降格動作，也可拿人不動作
6.如果沒人在起始玩家的話，轉完輪盤後補玉米在指針格。放起使玩家還是要算放總人數的玉米數

RATING: 7
Convert actions into resources into victory points in lots of different ways. The gimmick is the gear mechanism, which stops players going directly for the actions they want - they must place on the worst action of a type and each turn that they wait, they'll get a better action. So the constant decision process through the game is whether to take off now and do something else, or wait longer and get better - how long can you wait. The big strategy decision is how do you plan to collect your VPs, which then allows you to focus on which actions will get you there and not get distracted by the hundreds of other things you can do. So, yep, very Euro with no link to theme at all, but well done and seemingly tightly designed, with a great gimmick to bring it all home. Worth playing, probably worth exploring. But the game has such an incremental feel that it does seem to devolve to HR management with your dudes - on, off, on, off - and I'm not sure that replays will get me past that feeling, even when trying different strategies.

RATING: 6
What I like:
- Delayed gratification mechanic is cool
- You can win without taking additional workers
- 4p learning game finished in under 2 hours
- The asymmetrical starting goods/bonuses
- Appears to have multiple viable strategies

What I don't like:
- Too many options and combinations
- Timing is super-critical to the point of frustration

The theme doesn't mean a lot, but it doesn't feel abstract. It does feel a bit like a VP salad, although it rewards some specialization, and mostly encourages you to find your own path with less competition.

I would put the weight around 3.5, which is well above my preference. I would play this again, because it is quick and (at least in my first game) not very confrontational. But I would never ask for it.

RATING: 8.5
Based on one play: A very cool mechanic. You place your workers on dials and then each turn the main dial (calendar) turns, causing all your workers to move (or work). The longer you leave them, the better reward you get, since actions from your workers are taken when you remove them, not when you play them. All this to collect items to get end of game scoring or make offerings to the gods. A nice twist to worker placement.

RATING: 5

The gears...work..., but I cannot help but think the gears are an intended satire illuminating the worst part of eurogames.  I still enjoy many eurogames and probably always will, but Tzolk'in has at least showed me things I will try to actively avoid in games.  

Oh, and the fact that this game is ranked #17 shows me how utterly pointless rankings can be.  Totally undeserved.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game has a fantastic theme perfectly integrated with innovative mechanics.  Basically, you place your workers on a number of gears that all move in step with a central gear; the longer the worker stays on the gear, the better the action you'll get when you remove him (generally).  The catch is that, on your turn, you can either place or remove workers--never both and never neither.  Planning becomes essential in this game, as you don't want to get stuck needing resources from removing a worker the same turn you want to place, or run out of workers to place when you need your workers to keep going around.  If you plan it out right, you can truly have some amazing turns though (and conversely, it feels positively suboptimal to only do one thing on your turn sometimes).  This is an excellent planning game that rewards long-term thinking; definitely one of my favorites of the year.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 8.5
Excellent worker placement game. Introduces the element of "time", where if you wait before picking up your workers, you end up getting more. It offers strategy and tactical choices, as well as jockeying with the turn order; sometimes you'd want to be first, sometimes last. Plays great with 4 players and very good with 3 players. It's also playable with 2 players, but should not be played without expansions, since players will sooner or later find the dominant strategy of the game, which will kill its replayability.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Intricate game, with fairly simple and clear rules, but a ton of things going on. The amazing thing about Tzolk'in is that it's balanced in such a way that you can play it tactically or strategically, and it all works. I really does use time as a resource, or concept, which is fairly novel, and not at all gimmicky as it may appear.

At its heart, Tzolk'in is a game that requires planning. Every turn you either put one or more workers on the wheels, which will rotate to better positions if you let them stay there for a while, or move one or more of them off, and get rewards for it. You can't place and pull workers in one turn, so you need to plan ahead.

The genius is that it's up to you how far you want to plan ahead. You could play it purely tactically, opportunistically and reactively, put your workers in the best spots available, and pull them off as soon as they reach a desired spot. Or you could get in a three turn rhythm where you place two workers, then place two more workers, then pull all four and repeat. Or you could plan even more turns ahead, where you place a worker on the lowest spot, and decide that you won't touch it again until it has reached the highest spot five or more turns later. And then you could plan your strategy right from the beginning of the game, and decide where your roughly want to be placing for all twenty-something turns.

The balance = lots of options works for many aspects of the game. You could get to six workers, or stay at three, and because you won't be able to place them every turn anyway, these extra actions aren't game-breaking. Six or three can both be a valid strategy. Similarly, you could go for a lots of food strategy, which takes time to setup but gives you a lot of flexibility later, or just scrape by so you can focus on points from the get-go. Resources can be used for techs to help your overall strategy, for buildings for immediate benefits, or saved for monuments which score you a lot of points at the game end. Again, it's up to you how much you want to invest in future turns. Strategy or tactics, whatver you like best.

A nifty rule that sets it apart from ordinary worker placement games is that you have to place on the lowest available spot on a wheel. This means that when someone blocks you, as will often be the case in worker placement, this also opens up a new spot, the one that has now become the lowest available place. This means that on a micro-level, blocking isn't really an issue (and in fact, in this game one player places all workers before the next player does), but on a bigger level it certainly matters a lot whether a wheel is barely or heavily used by the players, so all the interaction is there.

Another thing that's great about the game, is though there is a lot going on on the board, everything is depicted in clear icons, so you shouldn't have any problem getting into the game again if you haven't played it for a year or so. 

Tzolk'in is certainly a modern game, in the sense that it has a billion stuff and tons of routes to victory. Lots of things score you points, and you can collect things that turn into other things, &c. More involved than elegant. More Feld than Knizia, in that way.

And that is a trend in boardgame design that I really dislike. But in Tzolk'in there's action, reaction, interaction. You make your own choices, but don't play your own little game. You manage, you plan, you respond to the demands of the game and to opportunities that open up. If you don't mind (or actively like) the planning that's needed, this is a ton of fun.

RATING: 8
It's resource management game where time becomes a crucial resource. Your turns are simple, as it's either place one or more guys, or pull one or more guys. Very easy for new players, but has a lot of depth in terms of trying to be the most efficient in your path. Each turn, the dials click, making the space your worker sits on more valuable, so the longer you wait, the better your action is. It's visually and mechanically fascinating, as you try to figure out the most efficient paths to collect the resources you need to win, while other players grab up your spots.

I should mention there is an expansion for this game that adds a 5th player, but I haven't enjoyed it so far, so I'm not going to go into it here further.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment

RATING: 6.5
Not a bad game at all, but has some really frustrating issues. First, the board itself was horribly warped, which made the main draw of the game (spinning the calendar wheel) seem like more of a chore than it needed to be.  Second, it was hard to shake the idea that every move needed to be long-term planned before taking an action.  There's just too much to do!  I'm sure this approach was meant to mirror the "point salad" Stefan Feld genre, but it feels more like busy work.  Further, because you must plan many moves ahead, AP becomes an enormous problem for all but the most decisive of players.  The tech tree is fairly superficial and feels tacked on as well.  
I guess my biggest complaint, however, is that despite the clever gears/planning mechanism and all of the bells and whistles, Tzolkin still feels like a fairly vanilla WP game.  I'd rather play Keyflower.

RATING: 5
An overly complicated worker placement game with some interesting ideas, but ultimately requires too much simple mental processing and too few interesting decisions.
The movement of the gears is just complicated enough that you can't easily see what the outcomes of your options will be, but simple enough that you can calculate the outcomes in your head for a turn or two in advance. This encourages, or really requires, you to constantly do boring calculations in your head to figure out how many turns from now you can take a set of actions together, or when you should place all your workers. I also just generally dislike games where you are best served by choosing one full-game strategy at the beginning and implementing it as efficiently as possible.
For all that complication, it doesn't feel like there are correspondingly more interesting choices to be made.  The available strategies are not really more nuanced than in simpler worker placement games, but the amount of calculation to make the strategy work is much longer and gets tedious. I would rather play a more straightforward worker placement game like Stone Age or Lords of Waterdeep.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Tzolk'in is one of those games that has a definite "aha" in its visual appeal. However, it is definitely not a gateway game because it has one of the steepest learning curves and it is very easy to miss a rule or to be overwhelmed by all the different moving parts. 

That said, the theme and mechanics combine well together and creating an "engine" that gives you a good mix of victory points and resources is the rewarding challenge. The replayability factor is high because the best engine for a given game will depend on the monuments in play (these vary every game and only a subset of all the monuments are used in a single game; monuments per game are a limited supply and not refreshed when a player builds one) and the buildings that come into circulation during the game (buildings, unlike monuments, are refreshed when a player builds one but even so not all buildings will see the light of day during a single game).

RATING: 9
This has to be one of the best - if not THE best - worker placement games out there. So they say the wheels are juest a gimmick, because you could do without them. Well, you probably could, but why would you? The way it's done, it's perfect - a perfect mechanic in a perfect game.

I love everything about it. Want to use more than one of your workers this turn? Here you go, use all of them if you want - but pay the cost. So simple, yet so engaging! Forces you to think strategically on multiple levels. How many workers do I need to stay on the wheels - and for how long? How much corn can I sacrifice? So many questions, and so many answers too! One of the games every eurogamer should own. Also, lots of replayability in the box (even more with the best expansion ever made, "Tribes and Prophecie"). You could play it a hundred times and still have fun with it. Seriously, just try it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
27-11-2017
After years of gathering dust on the shelf, recently I relearnt the rules of this one and ca. 4 weeks ago I put it on the table finally. That was partly due to the sudden emergence and rapid gain of popularity of the designer team in my house (Lorenzo Il Magnifico, Voyages of Marco Polo), so I was eager to test this, knowing that it was hugely popular on BGG. 
The distinctive feature of of the game is its worker placement/removal mechanics (I'm not 100% sure, but I think this was the first game where - as an action - you had to remove your workers from the table), and the rotating gears, which makes the yield of each worker more beneficial with every passing round. That is, if you can wait so long, until they get into these positions.... If you have no worker in your reserve, you do have to remove worker(s) from the board! This presents a very brainburny exercise of tempo play and timing: it is mighty satisfying when you managed to pull of your masterplan you started 4 rounds earlier!
Otherwise it is surprising, how the game managed to achieve so much with so little: there are not a lot of components in the box, but the underlaying web of mechanics yield a very complex game in spite of this. Due to this I regard this game as an example of a very elegant design. 
There is a lot going on in this game: aside of the main mechanics (worker placement / removal) there is the temple track, there are the monuments (varyiing end game scoring potential) and the different level 1/2 buildings, plus the requirement to feed your workers every so often. This is not that depressing as in Agricola for example but neither is it easy, so you definetely have ot put emphasise on this, otherwise you will face consequences during the feed days later on.
There seems to be a lot of valid strategy in this game and I'm eager to find them. :)

RATING: 8
Tzolk'in feels like a lot of other games, feeding the workers, building monuments, etc. What's really unique here is the way the gears change the pace of the game. Managing your resources is important, but managing your time is even more. It's fun to watch your workers make their way around the wheels, waiting for that one big action.

In the end, Tzolk'in takes my old favorite mechanics and breathes new life into them. It's a finely tuned game that I don't see myself ever growing tired of.

RATING: 9.61
The gears are great.  On your turn you can either place a worker(s) on the gears, or remove a worker(s) from the gears.  The longer you let them stay on the gears the more powerful the actions you get when you pull them off the gears.

Great Mayan theme.  Great artwork.  I'm too lazy to paint the gears, but you can make this game as visually stunning as you'd like if you're artistic.  Personally, I just printed an Aztec calendar on a color printer and taped it to my central gear....looks great.
