RATING: 8
A little complicated to learn, but very fun.
Description: Worker placement with delayed yield due to rotating gears, resource management, set collection, economic engine, farming Euro game placed in the ancient Mayan empire.

Players: 2 to 4. Best with 4.  Weight: Medium.

A standard economic game involving gathering and advancing resources, advancing on technology tracks and VP tracks, and creating buildings to improve your abilities and score points. Great production. 

Impression: Rotating gears representing Mayan calendar and a placeholders for workers are not just gimmick. It is one of the most brilliant game designs I've ever seen. There are multiple viable strategies and paths to victory and variable setup with starting tiles and randomized structures.

Likes: Gears. They are cool. Theme is also nicely implemented into mechanics and everything is well tied with said gears.

Dislikes: Existence of a dominant strategy that can be abused by the first player. I haven't seen it yet, but I have a knowledge of it and it is annoying. I presume it can be mitigated with Tribes and Prophesies expansion which I have yet to try.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Tzolkin is an excellently designed worker placement Euro. I really like the main mechanism of putting/taking workers on/off the board. This really elevates this from blander Euros, since basically you still gather resources, build buildings and advance on some abstract tracks.

The game really rewards additional plays as you become much better over time (it is about realizing on what to go for and what not to do). Especially when you discover how to go for the resource/building strategy and completely neglect farming, you can totally destroy any newbie players (well, destroy in the sense of gaining a lot more points). [i]Update: Well, you can destroy them  with farming+skulls, too; being experience just really matters  in this game.[/i] 

The components of the game are excellent. The gears work perfectly and make a really engaging game board. A real good bonus are the plastic skulls, which could just have been blue cubes. Upgrading the other resources with Stegmaier's Treasure Chest really  turns this game in a deluxe version.

RATING: 9.05
First off, the Mayan Calender wheel is an incredibly innovative design.  It automatically and effortlessly takes care of a ton of fiddly bookwork that would be needed it it were just chits or cubes moving around.  So if one is thinking it just a gimmick that is not true this game would be so clunky without it.  

That being said, this was a nice worker placement euro that really takes some planning and timing.  it really takes a game to start to click on this one.  Interesting decisions with respect to the technologies, Chichen Itzen wheel, God tracks and feeding your tribe.  

Bottom line, a fun game that really needs multiple plays to become efficient.  Lots of potential for this rating to rise as there is a lot more exploring possible in this game.

Update:this plays quick as a two player but still encompasses the same feel.  There is just so much to explore in this game so many paths.  Really enjoying it. However, i do wish I felt like I was building something more.  It's more of a "point salad" type game as the theme is a little thin (other than the innovative use of the Mayan calender).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.05
I was sold on the cogs. These five interconnected plastic cogs sit in the centre of the board. Every turn you advance the central cog one space and everything else moves as well. You put your workers on the cogs and then you pull them off when they reach the space that contains the resource you want (there essentially five different resources, you use corn to get workers on to the board, wood, gold, and stone to build things, and the anachronistic crystal skulls to score victory points).
This is really just a low interaction economic game where your placements and decisions subtly alter the market for the other players. But the cogs give it a real elegance and it seems to play quite quickly (we had three players) for a relatively deep game. I'm definitely keen to give this one another go.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Of all the new games of 2012, this was the one I most eagerly looked forward to playing. We got it early, in November oorc, and played it a few times when it was new, and I've played it about 6-8 times since. Over the course of these games, the initial promise of the game has faded away. In my gaming groups we just find it so hard to find a coherent strategy - forget those scores of 90 or 100+ you read about on BGG, I've never seen a score higher than the mid-60s, and scores of that range have almost always been achieved by the most straightforward route of going for crystal skulls. Every time I personally have tried a different strategy, I've crashed and burned, getting 40-odd, even less sometimes. I've even read strategy articles here, so know of the 3 supposed main strategies for high scores - but it's the tactics, and how to set these strategies up, which let me down. And no-one else in my gaming groups has managed better - even my wife, who is normally quick to pick up good strategies in games like this, and will crush the rest of us for at least a while. But not here, she certainly wins her fair share but gets scores no higher than the ones I mentioned earlier.

I know I shouldn't really penalise a game by rating it low (not that 7 is a low rating, but it's lower than I expected to be rating this game) just because I and the rest of my gaming groups are terrible at the game, but it can't help but affect the amount of fun I have playing. I'll still rarely refuse to play this, but it's almost never going to be a game I suggest any more. There are literally dozens of other Euros that I'd much rather play on almost all occasions.

To finish on a positive note, though - it's a very interesting and fun mechanism, and the game is very well presented as all CGE games are. As I already said, I'd almost never refuse to play it, so it maintains a 7 rating - its only real flaw is not being the amazing game that I had at first hoped for (and even expected).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A worker placement game in which the rewards for placing a worker correspond to the length of time you allow it to remain on the board and/or the number of workers already placed in the initial spaces of the same area.

Temporarily not having that worker to place elsewhere offsets the rewards of allowing it to remain in an area longer, since, in that time, the worker could have been placed in two or three different areas to reap a similar benefit, and the higher initial cost of placement offsets the rewards of starting in an area in which the first, less rewarding spaces have already been chosen.

My gut impression is that the players don't so much compete as they do inadvertently affect one another's progress. I only find it necessary to consider other players' resources and the actions they could take when I determine what structures I should work toward building. Otherwise, because every other resource aside from structures is unlimited, other player's actions don't prevent me from accomplishing what I intend to do.

Of course, it may be that I'm just not calculating enough.

RATING: 5
17-09-2015. Rated after 1 game. Tzolkin took me 3 years to play, and I understand why. It's a heavy euro, I don't usually play those.

The twist here is that there are 5 wheels, and during your turn you either put down markers or remove them from the wheels.  The longer they are on the wheel, the better the action. The earlier you place, and the longer you wait, the cheaper the action will be.
You score VPs in multiple ways. By bying tiles, offering to the gods etc. 
You also need to feed your people.

In any case, the game is ok. It's not my cup of tea but I can recognize a good design. It's a good game, but it looks drab and I cannot plan ahead.

RATING: 8
Take the most generic euro in existence and put in one gimmick. That's how it felt at first, but it turns out, that gimmick is a good one. The time aspect of getting the action you want puts a whole new spin on things and makes it really puzzley. 

Often, there are many ways to a single goal. The more you examine your options, you keep seeing more and more efficient paths. This is really heightened in the last few turns where you often want to do something to score big points, but need to fit it into the amount of turns left. I love the pressure that provides.

It could be just a little more punishing. The penalties for misjudging your moves are pretty lenient, though.

RATING: 6
I can see why this game is popular on BGG. It's the sort of thinky, analysis-paralysis-prone, indirect-interation-only, perfect-information puzzle that appeals highly to a certain (large) crowd.  If you like that, it's a well-done game with a pretty, albeit superficial, theme, and innovative mechanics that make time a crucial resource you need to plan around.  On the other hand, if you don't like having to have an exact plan two or three turns in advance, it can feel a lot like work.  Personally, I'm a fan of having some degree of randomness or hidden information to fuzz the planning horizon, letting you get by with heuristics instead of specific plans, and I prefer more direct interaction over just blocking other players, so it didn't do all that much for me.

RATING: 8
A bit dry, but forces you to do that wonderful strategic planning, where you have to know, almost exactly, what you'll be doing for the next couple of turns. Various strategies for obtaining points which you can't pursue all in a single game. It's a pity that one of them - advancing on the temples - is statistically the strongest, while at the same time being one of the easiest, fool-proof and luck independent, comparing for example to a more satisfying builder strategy. With fewer than four people you cannot afford to not fight for them.

Happily, this can by almost completely mitigated by not giving points for position on the temples at the end of first era (only the bonuses). It's a small change, with little impact on the game mechanics, but shifts the balance enough to make other strategies viable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Even without the calendar gears the game is a solid one. The gears are what really make it work and turn it into something special. While an elegant solution, they're actually a lot less complicated than I thought they would be and the whole process could have been effected through a simpler moving tableau. The gears are a mechanic I'd like to see developed in other games where players could choose different gear sizes to move on their turn rather than a simple movement of a large central gear per round.

So, what makes the game good? For my taste I enjoy the simplicity of the Carcassonne like mechanic - in this case you'll take any number of workers off the gears, or place as many as you like on the gears up to what you can afford. You're acquiring resources to pay for points you'll be able to earn in a multitude of ways - very Feld like.

This is a simple game with lots of interesting choices and paths, good interaction and manageable uncertainty. Because of the simplicity the game doesn't ever drag (I suppose it always could in the wrong hands), and you'll find yourself engaged and fully engrossed by the time it's over. A good sign. Replay ability comes from the mix of monuments, buildings and starting resources. Good stuff.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Worker placement game in which players collect resources to exchange them for victory points or developments. The design innovation is the gear, that allows to get better payouts depending on the time that a pawn has been placed on the board. So players try to get the best out of their possibilities not only managing resources, but also time, which is the most valuable of them all.

RATING: 4
The central mechanic is interesting, but it's poorly-executed. It's easy to see the train of thought that developed this game: "The Mayan calendar is hip right now. Ooh, gears! Corn takes time to grow, so the longer you wait, the more you should get. Shoot, I can't think of anything else intrinsically time-and-magnitude based, so maybe if you start carving a crystal skull, but it takes too long, it turns out you were actually mining gold while growing corn all along."

Tzolk'in would benefit from a complete rework. The idea that the passage of time gives you access to a succession of entirely-different, tangentially-related items and actions makes the design and layout seem entirely arbitrary. It'd be much better if the wheels were resource-generation only, and actions and purchases were entirely resource-limited, rather than being "right-place-right-time"-limited (which is just bizarre).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Adore this game. Amazing both visually and mechanically. The best part of this game is how it looks complex, but ends up being so elegant. Rewards good planning and strategy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Worker placement, but with the twist that payoffs increase the longer you leave your worker on the board. Gears are cool. It's nice to see out-of-the-box thinking. Perfect information, and the only source of luck is what building comes up after purchasing one.

A couple nice thematic touches too: I like that you can piss off the gods, and you need to cut trees before you can get (lots of) corn.

Also has my pet peeve with Euro designs: rules are too conservative and namby-pamby to the players. In this case, they create unintuitive exceptions. I can double-time the wheel to screw somebody... only if somebody picked Start Player... only worth it if we're 2 rounds away from feeding instead of both that and when we're 1 round away... can't do it if it'd squish somebody... why can't I squish somebody?!!

Strangely enough, I find this nearly the epitome of the 'pure' worker-placement game (e.g. Agricola isn't pure because of the cards, Stone Age has hidden/trackable information and more luck because of how the cards, not just the buildings, come up), but then the gears, which seem cool at first, disrupt the purity with some weird funky stuff that doesn't really belong to worker placement. So I liked the gears at first, then didn't like them because they just made things murky.

RATING: 8
After more than 25 plays of this, my rating for this game is pretty much solidified. The standout mechanism of Tzolk'in is undoubtedly the spinning gears, and I love the idea of options maturing the longer a worker stays on the board. There is so much else to appreciate about the mechanism ― it is a timing puzzle to determine when it is best to place or take back workers, and it enables Machiavellian moments of turning the wheel twice to everyone else's disadvantage. In an unconventional but inspired bit of design, it is sometimes in your favour for opponents to take the cheaper slots so that you can place workers in pricier, stronger slots.

While I initially found the game beyond the gears to be rather standard resource conversion fare, I now have a begrudging fondness for the supporting framework. Yes, it's not very original and primarily about racing up the temple tracks, but I've seen several distinctly successful strategies of racing up the temple (apart from the odd monument-building strategy). The point is that the supporting framework complements the gears nicely, ensuring that friction among players exists despite the presence of a few valid paths to victory.

What prevents me from rating this higher is that the chain of actions is rather overwrought for my liking. I found them overwhelming as a new player, and now find it rather stressful juggling the different elements as a semi-seasoned player. However, credit must be given where it is due ― many things could have easily gone wrong in Tzolk'in, but they didn't, and the end result is a finely-tuned design.

RATING: 9.75
#6 on my Top 100 Games of All Time (2016)
#8 on my Top 100 Games of All Time (2015)

Tzolk'in brought a new twist to the worker placement genre. Yes, the gears are really pretty and cool, so they may seem like a gimmick, but they work flawlessly in this game! The decisions you have to make throughout are engaging and challenging. This one definitely rewards long-term planning. The components are great, especially the crystal skulls. I quite like the expansion as well as it gives each player their own unique tribal ability. The base game already offers a lot of replayability thanks to the variable starting resources and buildings, but playing with the Tribes and Prophecies adds so much more! This one can feel a bit heavy, but it isn't too much of a brain burner. When I'm up for playing a longer Euro, this is towards the top of my list! It's available to play on BGA, but it's also available on Boite a Jeux with the expansion! Two thumbs up!!

RATING: 7.5
It's always great to see familiar concepts and mechanism evolve and that's just what's happened in this game.  I like to think of Tzolk'in: The Mayan Calendar as a dynamic worker-placement game since the actions available to a worker gets better the longer the worker remains on the gear.  This seems like such a simple progression in game design, but it adds so much depth and strategy, although the analysis can sometimes take a bit of time. 

My copy arrived with a significantly damaged board and it took a couple attempts and several weeks to get Rio Grande to properly replace it.  Not fun.  Apparently, they have since updated and changed the board a few times to reduce the rate of damage during shipping.

I have played Tzolk'in: The Mayan Calendar several times and finally have the rules explanation fairly streamlined. I like the game, but wonder if it will start to feel a bit samey after awhile. It is still a fairly basic worker placement game with the available actions improving over time. That's a nice twist, but the game is still just taking actions to get resources to gain advantages or other resources to get eventually get victory points. Some strategies also seem to be more obvious and work way better than others.  This is a concern.  I do look forward to playing more and will hopefully enough in it to keep me interested.  That having been said, I don't think that a full expansion is really necessary (outside the ones that just add a tile or two).

It is still too complex to hit the table regularly, but I can see enjoying this for a long time to come.

RATING: 8
[2012-11-03]
Az egyik legjobban sikerült idei esseni játékot próbálhattam ki. A dicséretek nem véletlenek a játék tényleg teljesen egyedi és nagyon jól működik. Féltem a szabálytól, de abszolút nem vészes. Gyakorlott játékosok körében egyáltalán kis gondot sem fog okozni, nem nehéz megérteni. A játékot akár rondellásnak is mondhatnám, de itt nem lépünk a rondellákon, hanem forgunk rajta minden körben egyet vagy esetleg kettőt, ha kezdő játékos úgy dönt. Az egyik keréken élelmet és fát, a másikon további nyersanyagokat tudunk szerezni. A harmadik kerék a fejlesztés és építés kereke, a negyedik a csere és gyarapodásé, az ötödik pedig győzelmi pontot hoz. A játék két korszakra oszlik, ezen időn belül kell munkásainkkal nyersanyagokat gyűjteni és épületeket építeni, illetve az ötödik keréken pontot szerezni. Körünkben összesen két dolgot tehetünk csak, vagy felrakunk a kerekekre a munkásainkból vagy leveszünk és akkor végrehajtjuk az éppen ott levő akciót. A játékba beleraktak egy a Caylusból ismert kegyrendszert és három templomot is, amelyen feljebb jutva kapunk különböző bónuszokat, illetve győzelmi pontot, ez szinte egy az egyben a Terra Mytica is hozta az idei évben, meglepő egyezőség. A játék a kerekek miatt szerintem egyedi, persze ezen kívül sok újat nem mutat, de úgy érzem ez elég változatosságot ad a játéknak. Egyetlen veszély a gondolkodás és a holtidő lehet a játékosok lépései között. Szerencsefaktor az egyes épületek feljövetelekor kerül csak a játékba, talán ez még belefér, bár néha lehet bosszantó is. Jó játék a Maya naptár, minden gémernek érdemes kipróbálnia.

RATING: 3
Super clever.  Great design.  But I just felt a constant annoyance playing it.  It has all the joy of waiting in a line mixed with all the work of a project manager.  I kept wondering why I wasn't getting paid my regular salary to play this.

This game features three of my least favorite elements in a game: 1. Lots of front end planning that is required to win, but can all be entirely disrupted before any of you plans can come to fruition (and you can't tactically respond).  2. Multiple dead turns in a row if you get out of phase, with very little to do of any interest. 3. Effective elimination where you know you can't actually play the game to come close to winning (or be in anything other than last place) and you just have to sit through the damn thing.  Yuck.

The gears are cool.  But I just didn't have any fun playing it.

RATING: 8
Most unique board mechanism. Delayed worker placement. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
2-4, 90m
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is the most original game I've played in a long time, and in a gamespace that is so crowded I wouldn't have thought I would see a new idea any time soon, that gamespace being worker placement.  I love worker placement games so this breath of fresh air caught me hook, line, and sinker!

The game plays by either placing or picking up your workers each turn on spaces on different gears.  Every turn those gears move forward 1-2 spaces and actions are performed when the workers are PICKED UP based on their position along the gears with the more valuable spaces being further along.  Hence, the longer a worker remains in play before being picked up the more valuable the action that worker takes is.

There are the obligatory feeding stages and the game is very tight.  It also requires a lot of forward thinking, even more than the previous "forward thinking" champ, Macao.  Being as you can either pick up or place workers each turn but not both you have to always be thinking when you want to pick up a worker, how many workers to keep in reserves, how much corn you need to place workers (placing workers on more valuable spaces or placing multiple workers a turn always costs this universal resource), feeding your workers, managing 7 different technology and temple tracks, and much more.  There is also building construction that breaks the rules of the game or gives you instant benefits.

There is so much to do and master that I don't see myself getting tired of this game for quite some time even if there isn't a huge amount of variety (there are a decent selection of buildings and powerful monuments but you get a shot at most of the buildings in just one playthrough).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Sometimes, as a player, I like to be inspired by games and by the different ways they use, like a disguise, to make the same thing as others. Tzolk'in kind of does that for me. The core of the game, if one takes off its clothes, is the exact same thing like in another zillion of games. But the way the game dresses itself for the moment reminds me of a good old fashioned movie in which the main female character is amazingly illuminated.

Played it only once but for now, and apart from the feeding engine (which doesn't seem to exist at some extent) I think Tzolk'in is a good keeper. Ahhh! But don't expect anything really different. It's a worker placement game with a "programming machine" in which you get resources to convert for Victory Points. But there's nothing wrong with that, right?!

(N/G)

Hoping this will be one of the best Euros of Essen 2012

Another to like about the game is that getting more workers is not essential to do well

RATING: 7.5
Worker placement somewhat reminiscent of The Manhattan Project -- you either place one or more workers or pull back one or more workers. The trick is that actions are only taken when workers are pulled back, but they are placed on moving gears that correspond to a different (usually better) action each turn. The game timer also includes four "feeding days", with penalities for not paying corn for each worker as well as special scoring opportunities. Managing these overlapping timers in the face of stiff competition is where the fun is, though in practice it's less of a brain-burner than it sounds: the shared gears usually make the board too chaotic for mid-range planning.

There is nothing particularly notable about the rest of the game; it's the usual Feld-ish grab-bag of subsystems.

These days I prefer a more minimalist approach, not exactly a CGE hallmark -- I strongly feel that the game was developed with an eye to fixing balance by adding more, rather than taking anything away. It's a Frankenstein's monster, edge cases bolted on all over. A few of the bits, such as the second level harvest tech, I don't think are quite right. As always, I also dislike the (unnecessary) luck, such as the building draw.

But of greatest practical concern is that a combination of complexity, changing placement costs, blocking, and 3-6 actions per turn (where order is important!) can make it difficult to plan during other players' turns. With the wrong people this is fertile ground for AP downtime hell. My first game went for four hours, and while that is certainly the fault of an unholy confluence of the wrong players, the game does encourage longer turns rather than micro-actions that keep the pace up. Bring a timer, or maybe a gun.

Despite minor niggles like dying of old age while waiting for my turn, it's a pretty good game. I'm not sure it's a [i]great[/i] game.

RATING: 8
[RGG 2012 edition] {Mayan; resources-management, worker-placement; optimal 4p} This may not be the best worker-placement Euro game so far (and there are already many ones).  However, I quite like this game because I like the Mayan civilization and this game has a good theme.

As a strategic game, this game offers multiple paths of scoring: temples, monuments, Chichen Itza, etc.  It is a game of timing - one has to plan carefully how to use their workers and to use a turn very efficiently (as it is wasteful to place / pick one work at a time).  Resources (wood, stone, gold, corns and skulls) are tight in this game, one has to plan in advance in order to collect different resources efficiently.

The game is interactive and I would like to play it again soon.

The components are nice:
- the player aid is quite handy, but I wish a cue card to explain the technologies is available;
- the crystal skulls are nice looking;
- the gears are gimmick (at least my daughters like them).

However, it would be even nicer if the worker are made human pawn like (rather than a plain cylinder).
And, it would be nice if a little booklet describing Mayan history is included :P

RATING: 9.2
The cogs, many of you have heard from Tom Vasel (dice tower reviews) that are nothing more than auto advancing on 5 tracks, it is TRUE, but it is VERY IMPORTANT, and i cannot stress that enough.

The thing is, that i stopped playing fungi (morels) because on each turn i had to move 7-8 cards, just to be able to play anything, and if the cogs weren't there, that means that on each turn every player should have been moving up to 6 workers on up to 5 tracks, which would made me stop playing that game. It would be stupid, I want to play game, not work a mindless office job.

That being said, the cogs advance everything that needs advancing per turn, and there is 0 actual downtime due to "technical" stuff, you get the full time from playing the game actually playing the game. IT IS AWESOME how fluidly the game goes.

Another thing I like in this game is that you either take or put workers, you cannot do both, which further more increases the flow of the game, because a lot less thinking is involved AND you sometimes even notice that it is your turn, because the player before you started taking workers and doesn't have any more to take, so you don't even need to hear him saying "I'm done".

I do like the feeding mechanic of the workers, but it feels more like you don't give them food at work, rather then they are starving and don't have what to eat.

I don't like that they haven't put enough tiny baggies for all the different types of components, because without them separated the setup time is increased which is completely unnecessary.

RATING: 9
Very clever worker placement game packed with options and routes to victory. The game is driven by the placement of workers onto one of five gears interlocked with a central gear which moves every turn. When the workers are removed from the gears they yield whatever they were facing on the board, be it food, resources, victory points etc. There's so much going on here that the number of choices available and how best to use them is genuinely impressive and players have to decide whether to go straight for victory points, construct buildings making the game easier, improve technologies making their worker options more impressive, feed their workers to avoid penalties or to advance through the three temples to gain resources and victory points later in the game. The gears are anything but a gimmick and everything comes together so well making this a clever, demanding and rewarding game that feels quite innovative.

RATING: 9.5
tl;dr: still excellent, still unique

This game introduced a new, novel and unique mechanism with the gears that advance the placed workers from one action to the next. Usually when a game introduces something new and unique, it gets copied and reworked and introduced into games the original designers couldn't imagine. As a gamer, I love it when designers let us experience these mechanisms in different ways in different kinds of ways. For this game though, that didn't happen. I understand why though: this is not cheap to produce, but still.
In any case... this game doesn't just have a unique mechanism and use it as a gimmick. No, it's a very solid strategic game in it's own right with a bunch of different strategies to explore. I like it a lot!

Score: 9.5/10
BGG scale: 9/10

RATING: 7
Worker placement, but the workers don`t perform the actions immediately. ON your tirn you pick the workers on the gear or from the gear. When you take them from the gear they perform the action. After a turn all gears roll, and the workes on the weels move to a usually stronger (sometimes only different) action.

So the game is a worker placement where you perform the action with a delay. You cannot wait until the worker moves to the last (best) spot, because you must make an action (pick up from the gear,or put on the gear), you cannot pass.

In every game you randomly choose starting tiles, buildings and monuments. Is this enough for high replaybility? In our game 2/3 players buy only few buildings, and they don`t had a big impact on the game.

Won`t play more. When the replaybility will be high, my rating will go up.

RATING: 6
It's a worker placement game with relatively straightforward paths to victory. Yes, it has gears. Yes, there are timing issues to manage that make the game somewhat interesting. But it's not so amazing as to mask the somewhat slow gameplay and "just another worker placement game" feel. In all honesty, I don't think the designer took full advantage of the gear mechanic. It's not "mean" enough. Only on rare occasions do you move past your desired outcome, and, when you do, you can typically pay to claim the space before. It's a neutered mechanic that deserves to be the center of a tightly focused game about truly tough decision making. Instead, it's just pleasant and clever.

Trajan, another game published in 2012, also involves timing your moves (in a different way, but similar), and manages more excitement. Tzolk'in is okay -- I'd play it on occasion -- but I didn't find it as amazing or innovative as some people seem to.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Tzolkin came out at a time when I was completely burned out on worker placement and euros in general, so for whatever reason I never had a desire to play it.  But now after a few years, I'm glad I gave it a try.

Tzolkin does so many things right.  Your choices are simple (place guys or remove guys), yet always complicated.  The gears are a great mechanism, visually pleasing, and fun to turn.  The research track is excellent and allows you to form satisfying long term strategies.  The way that you feed your people remind me of Agricola (which I love), yet it's not quite as much of a struggle.  Perhaps my favorite part is that you start with 4 tiles and choose 2 to determine your starting resources/research/buildings.  It's a simple, euro version of variable player powers, which is one of my favorite mechanisms and gives you a direction to follow in your strategies.

I can't really think of anything negative to say about the game.  It captured my attention for the entire game.  I definitely want to give this more plays, and I could see it becoming one of my favorite euros alongside Agricola, Power Grid, and Puerto Rico.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
It's not often that I find a game both innovative and enjoyable, but Tzolk'in: The Mayan Calendar is both. The gear mechanics moving your workers to different action spaces each turn truly is a novel twist that differentiates this game from the host of other worker-placement games out there. Best of all, the gear mechanic is neither a shallow, short-lived gimmick (Niagara, anyone?) nor does it have a fiddly, puzzle-like feel. Instead, it is a clean design that adds some tension and timing to a well-developed game where both strategy and tactics are needed. After four or five games, I have some initial concerns that players fortunate enough to buy the best food buildings early have a tremendous advantage, and that perhaps the placement of the skulls is a stronger method of gaining points than building monuments, but I need more play to determine if this is the case. Other than those possible balance issues, the game is novel, entertaining and challenging. UPDATE: Allowing one player to deliver skulls uncontested absolutely lets that player crush everyone else ... all players simply need to be aware of this. The game continues to get table time on a regular basis and I've bumped my rating up to 9.

RATING: 9
Feeling very good about this after just one play. It does a lot of things that I like in a game: technology tracks that give you permanent bonuses; unique worker placement; lots of different buildings that give instant bonuses; different starting resources for each player, every time you play. It also seems to be good with two players, and the rulebook is really well done. Will give this a rating after more plays but expecting it to be a 9 or 10.

Update: Giving this a 10 for now. This game gives me the feeling that it combined some of my favorite games into something special. Worker placement and feeding your workers like in Agricola, building structures like in Caylus, chaining together actions like in Castles of Burgundy. Only the punishment for not feeding workers isn't as severe as Agricola, which is a good thing for me.

I also want to add more about the rulebook. This should serve as a model for how to make a great rulebook. Everything is explained clearly, there are plenty of pictures and examples, there is a nice game flow guide that you can follow for your first few turns to make sure you're doing everything correctly, and the back of the rulebook serves as an illustrated reminder of all of the symbols in the game. Very well done.

Update: Seems like the temples might be overpowered. Sure, there are different methods to advance on the temples, but usually the person who does best on the temples wins by a good margin. Will have to play more to see if this holds up, and if so, hopefully the expansion will address it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I rate it an 8 after one play. I suspect it could rise with more plays. Take into account that I'm invested in the game because I painted all the wheels before I put it together. It took me about 6 hours of work, but I'm very happy with the finished product.

As for the game, I feel there's a lot to discover there. I took second place in my first play, but I think I could have won with a couple more turns.

The game can be very unforgiving for those who don't want to carefully plan their turns several rounds ahead--or for those who don't do that well. There's a lot of different paths to victory, and I'm excite to play it more to figure some of those paths out.

Also, it should be said that the first play of this game will take at least an hour per player with set-up, rules, and take-down. There's a lot of little things to explain. And you can't just jump right in because important decisions are made by every player even during set-up (tiles are distributed to players and they choose 2 of 4. Those tiles determine each players' starting resources/rewards, which in turn, help determine strategies from the very beginning.)

I look forward to playing it more, and I love that I've painted it, learned it, played it, and will continue to play it with the Mayan people while living in Guatemala. In fact, we're visiting Tikal in three weeks. Very thematic for me.

8/2/2013
This game is hard (for me, anyway). I haven't played it once where I felt like I could realize any solid strategies. My wife always seems to jump out early, and keep rolling ahead. It's a bit frustrating at times. But I suppose that's a good thing in a way; it's a challenge for me to master. I'm going to give it several more plays to see if I get any better. For now I'll keep it because it's such a challenge for me to play well. Hopefully I'll be keeping it because I really enjoy playing it. :)
[COLOR=#FFFFFF]TAGS: [/COLOR]

RATING: 9
After my initial play I am very impressed. I love games that successfully manage to feel new and unique. This one definately succeeds. The gears used for progressing workers turns out to be amazingly well balanced because of the built in opportunity costs around placement vs. removal for actions vs. timing for ideal actions. That you can only either place or remove in a single turn is in and of itself ingenious. This is definately not a light game if you want to stay competetive. The player interaction is limited to blocking, but is still very effective. Timing is crucial in this game. While there is some amount of engine building, it is not as dominant of an element as in Caylus or in the Harvest Series games. I will be playing this a few more times over the next couple of days but do not expect my rating to change from a 9.

RATING: 8
A solid and unique eurogame.

The game offers simple choices and short enough rules yet quite tough decisions. This leaves room for strategic thoughts halfway through the very first game, which is rare for a euro. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
What I like: 

-The wheel is very innovative and fun! The fact that time and space has become something to account for makes this very unique and fun, and requires a different set of thinking.
-Standard well-produced worker-placement that looks pretty good too. 
-Enjoy that they managed to get it down to 90min of play, which isn't hard to acheive for the players either. 
-I like the deceptive simplicity of just removing or placing workers each round.

What I dislike:

-Nothing changes during the game. What I mean by that is that you don't really get to experience any growth during the game. You are always just place or remove workers. They don't really get stronger or better or different during the game. Yeah you can get more, and become better at some of the wheels, but that is usually just done early game otherwise you aren't being cost efficient with your actions.
-The strategies aren't balanced. Some of the upgrades for the different wheels are just terrible choices and it's hard to know that before you have tried and failed with them. (I strongly recommend AGAINST upgrade the ressource wheel except maybe the first for extra wood), and generally a strategy focused on just building is going to be very poor. 
-The final score is a bit odd. This game is all about climbing on the God tracks. The game rewards you on being highest on each of these 3. This seems okay, but what it usually means is that even though you've had an extremely close game, the one player who gets just a bit ahead on the tracks is going to end up with a massive lead, making it seems like he or she was way ahead. WHereas in reality this just reflects that slight edge on the God Tracks.

RATING: 9.3
Awesome game. 

Every game played feels significantly different thanks to different selections of available buildings and different starting tokens for each player.  There's numerous viable strategies, different engines that you can build and a lot of adaptive reasoning and planning ahead is required to make the best moves at the best times.

The game is not easy to explain to new players and there's a lot of things going on at once.  I'd recommend this game primarily for people who are already experienced in heavy strategy games.  The games can be long, and it can be difficult to recover if you start out by making some bad moves.  

The components, gears and board are all really well designed and nice looking.  The gears on my board got a bit warped and no longer link together properly unless I push them down, which is a bit of a component issue, but not the end of the world.

Overall, great game, highly recommended for players who enjoy heavy strategy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
[url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/126163/tzolkin-the-mayan-calendar]Tzolk'in: The Mayan Calendar[/url] is, simply, the best euro from 2013 so far, to me. Well, let me be honest: it is the [b]best game overall[/b].

Last year, when the talks about it started, I thought it was just one more game trying to make money by drawing attention to a propitious moment (the end of the world!). And, as most of these, it would be a empty game. Nothing more than a gimmick. But then, lo and behold, people started to say good - very good, even - things about [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/126163/tzolkin-the-mayan-calendar]Tzolk'in: The Mayan Calendar[/url]. They said that the gears were really useful and smart, not just some eye-candy; that there is, indeed, a solid game in the rules.

And, at last, I had the opportunity to try it first hand. And, yes, it is awesome. Everything on it works together. The actions are intuitive, but, at first, the explanation of the game was scary - there are so much things to be aware of, but even though is necessary to know all of them, they came only one at a time, by the spin of the wheels.

You can gather food and wood; make developments; construct buildings and wonders; pay homage to the Gods; acquire resources - wood, stone, gold, crystal skulls. All of this you can do, and all of them are interconnected and are useful - but you won't be able to use one of the actions in a rinse-and-repeat type of play: you will need food, resources, developments, buildings and appease the Gods.

There is so much to do, and so beautifully connected, that after the game ends, you want to play again, to try new things, ways, to use more of something else.

[url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/126163/tzolkin-the-mayan-calendar]Tzolk'in: The Mayan Calendar[/url] is, for me, in the same league as [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/boardgame/18602/caylus]Caylus[/url] - or, to translate this: in the top tier of the work-placements.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
First game will take longer than expected, This game is a 6-7 in the first play but a 8-9 with experience.  

Some turns are very quick some can take a while, so be in the mood for a though provoking game with some very unique mechanics.  

Not for the uneducated gamer, as they will be smothered in the background.  But if you are a gamer this game has a lot of staying power and will get more efficient and better with multiple plays.  

Also it is a good idea to have an understanding of the buildings and the monuments that are in the game before you play your first game.  it is not a "lets be surprised or figure it out as they come up" type of game. 

Strategies must be discovered early, and it is difficult to change up as time goes on.  

By the way Corn is by far and away VERY important, don't try to jump on late game heroics early, or you will be dead in the water, or Beheaded by the gods as was the way in the Mayan times...!

RATING: 10
After the first couple of plays, love it, curious to play more and get better at it. The look of the game is very nice, the gear mechanism works perfectly, and I'm a sucker for this particular (pasted on, but that's fine with me) theme.

After more plays: Yeah, we still love this. Very competitive, still haven't mastered it. One of my top five, for sure.

RATING: 7
At its heart, Tzolkin is a worker placement game. The core game is simple to grasp: You either place workers of remove workers during your turn. The interlocking wheels and gears then advances the track over time such that the actions your workers can take become more valuable over time. How long then can you delay your gratification for the workers you place on the wheel?

While the wheel is kinda a gimmick, it does fit the theme quite well and there is no other game out there that has a comparable feel to it. This is high praise given the amount of worker placement games out there. I believe there are different strategies to pursue and I can see both short and long term tactics and strategies for this game. I also can't help but think that optimum strategy lies in being opportunistic in worker placement but also timing everything just right to execute a series of moves to maximize points.

An excellent game worthy of my collection. The only drawback, really is the length of play. This game takes a lot longer than is expected.

Rather interesting and balanced euro game with cool gear-system-twist!

RATING: 9.5
Simplesmente o melhor "Worker Placement" q joguei até agora, e pelo q eu senti, talvez o melhor q eu vá jogar (e sim, eu já joguei Agricola). A princípio um pouco assustador, pela quantidade de símbolos e engrenagens, mas assim que vc começa a ler o manual e entender o jogo, percebe a simplicidade e inteligência contida alí.
Os símbolos, a princípio muito variados (mais que 7 Wonders) logo começam a fazer sentido de uma forma bem intrínseca, mérito para a arte.
Com um mecanismo simples, mas muito original, e que se integra perfeitamente com a temática do calendário Maya, esse é um jogo na medida certa: tempo, opções de jogada, equilíbrio, diversão e tensão.
E é um jogo bem pensado mesmo para jogar com menos de 4 jogadores, até mesmo com apenas 2, o que não é comum em muitos jogos. E uma das coisas mais fundamentais: fator sorte praticamente zero!
Não é perfeito, talvez não tenha tanta rejogabilidade, e facilitaria se os "Wealth Tiles", buildings e monuments fossem cartas ao invés de tiles, facilitando o embaralhamento. A pontuação em volta tb poderia ter números (não só de 5 em 5) para facilitar a leitura. Mas esses "contras" são meros detalhes técnicos, de implementação do jogo, e não tiram em nada o seu mérito gigantesco. JOGAÇO!

RATING: 8
This game is definitely "easy to learn, hard to master" and it succeeds in being my favorite worker placement in a genre that I normally am not too keen on.  It's the quickness of gameplay and flexibility of the board that win me over, among other things.

I really love the temporal aspect of the game, the wheels are far from a gimmick, they eliminate fiddliness and aid the user in planning.  I'm not a big fan of worker placement games, but the way this board is laid out, it really makes this one a bit easier to follow.  Everything is laid out in front of you, you see the last turn counting down, you can easily count turns on the gears.  There's no math you need to work out with multiple workers.

Your moves could be as simple as just taking or placing one worker, but every move has huge impacts on the game and future turns.

This game is the first time I've been inclined to really invest in a game of this complexity long enough to really dig into it's inner workings until it "clicked", I enjoy it more and more with each play.  

Possibly my favorite part is how seamlessly the dummy-worker mechanic allows you to play at any player count.  The only reason why I don't play it too often and why it's not even higher on my list, is because it is a bit of a brain burner so I have to be in the right mood.  

My one complaint about the base game is that it does run out of variability, you can reach a point of overplaying where you start setting up systems that you follow.  The expansion "fixes" this with more variable player powers and the prophecies.

**Update** The expansion fixes my one complaint, the game is now amazing.

Base game: 8
w/ Expansion: 9

Game is finally falling off my interest, though 24 plays is nothing to sneeze at.  Still a solid game, but I'm  mostly onto new things for now.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
My single play of this was far from the ideal situation, but I don't see a lot of reason to come back to this game.  The gear components have a nice wow factor, but it's in service to a game that doesn't have much else of interest.  It's a lot of same ol', same ol' convert resources into each other and build stuff.  Mechanically, little is new here -- the worker placement and feeding mechanics are ripped straight from Agricola, the buildings and resources mechanics have been done in oodles of games, and even the gears are just a flashier version of the resource delay system found in [gameid=55670].

While there's a few nods to the theme in terms of named dials and some numbers, there's very little to connect the gameplay to Mayan culture.  It could be seamlessly converted to Old Thyme Builders in Europe without losing anything, and that's never a good sign

RATING: 5
Like many modern euros, my first play with four was 'interesting' but the game subsequently remained unplayed on the shelf for nearly a year. After a second play with two, it was again 'interesting, but don't care if I never see this again'.

It's unthematic, a bit dry, and there's no much draw to bring you back to the table if gaming is about more than 'getting the most VPs'. The game mechanics actively discourage player interaction since you can avoid being blocked by paying corn to jump ahead on the cogs, and get extra resources next turn by doing so.

On the upside, the cogs deal with most of the bookkeeping and it's easy to get on the table. So, if you like dull VP optimisation exercises, it's among the best of its type. Especially good for people who enjoy thinking several turns in advance and puzzling out long-term strategies.

RATING: 6
I was afraid that this might be more gimmick than game, but the gear mechanism actually works pretty well.  It's a timer and a tactical element to the game, and it's a good fit for a worker placement game.  There are a lot of things to keep up with and understand in the game, but like most CGE games, all the information you need is represented well on the board.  Teaching or learning the game will take about 30 minutes, though, and it will take a bit for the players to get up to speed.  Once you understand the iconography, it's easy to remember what each bonus and space does for you.

The downtime might be a bit severe as the game progresses, as players try to figure out how to accomplish their plans in the fewest moves, but it seems like it would be about the same as in Agricola, so it doesn't bother me.  Plus, we haven't played a complete game yet, so it's hard to judge that right off the bat.  Still, I want to play it again, so that's a good sign.

.5 plays: 4 players

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Now this is how you shake the label of "typical stodgy Euro". While gameplay is nothing more than the usual worker placement/resource swap for VP's, the method of delivery is one of the most unique in gaming. The board is made up of interlocking gears, which turn and keep your workers in constant motion. Besides being a very clever mechanic, the big plastic gears are also a hell of a toy. They don't do anything but spin, but when this game hits the table it is guaranteed to draw a crowd.

All of that aside, the game design is quite solid. The iconography is easy to learn and more importantly, consistent. Once you learn to decode a half-dozen tiles or so, the rest just falls into place. Strategically, this feels like a Feld game. There are lots of easy to recognize methods of swapping resources for victory points, which let even a beginner get into the groove and score some points. Of course, the game allows for some huge point scoring opportunities for those that can think three turns ahead and have all of their workers fire off in one action.

I have heard some chatter that the strategies are one dimensional. I don't see that yet in my games, but I certainly understand how it might happen. Still, I think it will take a lot of games to fully unwrap it, not to mention the expansion does a few things to combat that issue. We are diving right in with the expansion, so maybe it won't ever end up like that for us. I'll check back in after a half dozen or so more games...

RATING: 6
2 plays. Something is off in the economy of this game.

Further thoughts, now that I know what I like and what I don't like, in particular, about these core Euro games.

I hate the observance for all its moving parts that gets in the way of just *playing* one-another, as in, directly considering the actions, movements and intents of other players and combating them. In Euro games, you have this "just do what everyone else isn't doing" except you sit there and extrapolate what your potential VP net is and how much time you have to achieve it. If you're really good, you'll do the same for everyone else and realise that you either do or don't have enough time, or that too many elements obscure the truth about your potential net VP, so you play out the game often thinking you're doing well only to realise you were 2 turns behind but there was no way for you to forecast it.

That all comes down to poor overall design, but I'm careful when I say that - I acknowledge that designing a game is super difficult, and I applaud anyone who wants to give it a decent shot, it's not easy at all. Nevertheless, I realise that rules overhead and complexity don't always make a great game, especially if the designers can't make all strats viable while also facilitating interaction and pivoting.

I think the sheen of "just doing anything and getting points" has for the most part worn off for me, especially when so many strats on offer will never be truly viable and there's so much channeling.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Tzolkin, a worker placement where the value of the work your workers do evolves over time. As the game proceeds players advance/rotate the gears moving the workers to different action spots. Actions located "later" on the gears are more valuable, so waiting can be good, but being able to pay your workers in corn is more important. 

On their turn players can either place 1(+) workers claim the work from 1(+) workers. The game ends after a revolution of the central gear. To allow for many paths to victory, and variable starting setup for each player, the game has to be a bit fiddly. There is a lot on the board, but it comes together pretty well. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Phenomenal strategic euro game with a unique worker placement mechanic - I love it! Tzolk'in is all about preparation and long-term planning. It's not easy to time your actions perfectly, but if you manage to do so, it will be a very satisfying experience. If you like brain burning games with numerous paths to victory - this one is for you. Keep in mind that the gameplay can be pretty stressful though - you are constantly fighting with time and the shortage of resources. 

[i]I own the Tribes & Prophecies expansion, Tribes & Prophecies Mini Expansion 1 and Mini Expansions 1 & 2.[/i]

RATING: 8.3
I finally had the chance to try this game and I am quite satisfied with the experience.
First the bad things: there aren't many. I just have the slight impression that the game has a limited replayability BUT I could be wrong since there are so many ways you can play your game and this should add variety. What else? well, it's another resource to VP conversion game, so if you are bothered by this... you are warned.
Now the good things: the best aspect is the very good flow of the game. It is fast fast fast and i very much appreciate it. Your action is just "put workers" or "remove workers" and you do this 26 times or so and time goes by very nicely; you are left with the wish of going on playing it again.
It's original in its worker placement mechanic with gradual power up of the workers themselves.
It's well architected: you have to use a very good timing, you have difficult decisions, many strategies to follow. 
It's not very complicated. It seems complicated at the beginning because the board is filled with many things but after a decent explanation everything is clear.
Components are among the top 5 german games i have ever seen. Publishers did an amazing job and every detail has been taken care of in the best possible way. The cogs are so nice, they fit wonderfully well and usually (not always) they do not get stuck.
You obviously need a couple of games to understand how to play it well but since the first game you can enjoy it quite a lot.
All in all, a very good experience: you must try it soon or later.
Why not a better rating? well, I don't like games that are too rich and that contain too many things so i need a few more games to get used to it and "digest" it completely: but the rating can't do anything else but rise.
Good job Daniele and Simone!

RATING: 7
Good game, but pretty fiddly if you ask me. Could it be, have I hit the worker placement wall?  Games like this one have to grow on you.  Most people will have no idea what to do the first few times.  The problem with this (because this isn't always a problem) is that you see no results doing what you are doing unless you get and place crystal skulls (which is only one way to get points).  So the learning curve is huge.  And then there are exceptions to getting things which make things confusing.  So I get the game.  I get why people like it.  And I get how this game would get better with more plays.  But I think there are better worker placement games out there, especially for the new player.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A worker placement, resource conversion sort of game but the twist is that workers are placed on cogs, which rotate as the game progresses, allowing access to better actions. Underneath the cogs it's not hugely different from other resource conversion games but worth taking a look at.

On your turn you can either place workers or remove workers, not both. If you have no workers in your supply, you must remove some from the board and vice versa. When placing workers, they must go on the first available space and the idea is to leave them on the cog until it has spun around to the action you want. There's a food cog, a building materials cog, a "build stuff and advance your technologies" cog, a points scoring cog and an "other stuff" cog.

There are harvest rounds where you have to feed your workers or lose points; there are building tiles you can build for points or other benefits; there are four technology tracks you can advance on to augment your basic actions; and there are three temple tracks on which you can jockey for position with the other players. The temples pay out points and/or resources in harvest rounds. If you've played Stone Age, Agricola or Caylus, you'll have a good idea of what much of this game is like. But what makes it different from those games is the cogs.

I can imagine the designer playing Agricola and thinking "what if I could leave my Family Member on that 3 Wood space for an extra round and get 6 Wood next turn instead?" That's what this game does, but your worker doesn't block the space while it's waiting there. The cogs are an elegant way of implementing this idea and there's a good bit of tension between wanting your worker back for use elsewhere and wanting to get a better reward by keeping it there. I think the cogs are great, but I was a little disappointed they weren't put to more use. I'd have liked to see the ratios between different sized cogs being a bigger feature, or an option to move to a different sized cog within the same group of actions, or even the option of moving from one cog to a smaller connected one.

There's a subtle and interesting interaction between players regarding where they place their workers on a cog. You have to play on the lowest available space (often the 0 space), but that means you'll be waiting longest to get the good actions. You can start off on a higher space only if all the spaces before it are already occupied and even then you have to pay some extra food for the privilege. As the start player is the one to rotate the central cog each turn (which correspondingly rotates all outer cogs by one space), they will always have the 0 space free. This can be a good thing if they wanted to get on a certain cog but didn't mind whereabouts, but it can also be a disadvantage if they wanted to start higher along the cog - they’d have to place on the 0 space before they could place further ahead too, and placing multiple workers in one turn costs more food. To counter this, using the Start Player action lets you pick who will be the Start Player, as you might not automatically want it to be you. Sometimes you hope an opponent leaves their worker on a cog so that you can place above them on your turn. Other times you might want them to vacate a space so you can access that cog at a lower cost. There are a lot of moving parts to this game and not just the cogs on the board.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Theme/Art:
I like the theme a lot, I was always interested in Aztec and Mayan culture. The theme also fits the game and the game looks great and colourful.
The art is great too, especially the box art.

Production quality:
Very good, the board itself is very unique and the gears work very smoothly. Resources are basic wooden cubes, aside from wood and corn, which are cardboard tiles, and crystal skulls, which are little plastic skulls. Everything else is cardboard.

Replayability:
Since Tzolk'in has a lot going on and quite a few ways to score points, replayability is given. Additionally the order of buildings and the available monuments are different every game.
The expansions adds even more replayability with different tribes and prophecies.

Depth/Difficulty:
The core mechanic of the game can be explained in one sentence: On your turn you either place workers on gears or take workers off gears to receive the benefit of the gear space you took them from. However, there are a lot of different gear spaces to learn and there are also gods, technologies, and harvest festivals.
I weight it 4/5.

Strategy:
You need to think ahead, manage your resources, keep workers on gears as short as possible, but as long as needed to get the best benefits. It plays very fluid though, because turns are very short.

Luck:
Pretty much non existent. Aside from starting tiles, buildings, and monuments nothing in this game is random.

Tzolk'in is an amazing game and depending on player count it takes 60-150 minutes to play.

I think the gears are a great and unique way to implement a worker placement mechanic and I can't wait to get the expansion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Throughout the years of playing this game, I have found that this is still one of my top games of all time.  I have literally played this game hundreds of times and even though my thoughts on the strategy of this game have grown, developed, and changed, my rating has only increased (started out as a 9).  This is an excellent game. I love how there are so many things going on in the game at one time.  There are so many things to take into consideration when you're making your placements or removing your workers. At the beginning of the game you have to look at the monuments, choose your strategy, and try your best to execute it.  The game revolves around three main methods of strategy: corn, skulls, or buildings.  You can win and score big pursuing multiple combinations of the three, but I have found that it is best to stick with one.  Buildings are widely known to be "the best" overall choice because you can score the highest points by pursuing them.  Throughout my plays, I have found this isn't true.  Skulls are definitely a viable option and I regularly beat building players by pursuing a skulls strategy.  I will say that this is a game where if another player is pursing the same strategy as you are, both of those players are going to suffer.  Also, start player is a big advantage because you can get the fourth worker right away (which is huge).  I do feel like a variant for balancing start player would make the game tighter and slightly more balanced.  With these criticisms you may think that the game would be rated lower, but the fact is that it isn't because the overall design, engaging gameplay, and unique gear shifting mechanic, make this an exciting and unique worker placement experience that feels unique and creative.  The technology upgrades are something that you don't usually see in a worker placement game. The game feels very tight but at the same time, you always feel like you have options that will benefit you.  A smart player is always reading their opponent.  Sometimes you don't place a worker(s) in a spot(s) because it only will serve to benefit your opponent's overall strategy.  In early games, you might feel like the temples are the determining factor of who wins.  The more you play, the more you see that developing your long term strategy, whether that involves temple pursuit or not, is the most effective form of victory.  You should always pursue more workers, and try to advance the  technology that will suit your end game strategy ASAP.  Don't hesitate to buy this game.  It is an amazing game design that I would love to see other designs emulate.  

Note: Plano Box

RATING: 8.3
This game was a hype, even before it was released. Only because of the gear mechanism. When the big wheel has made one full turn (this will take a while), the game ends. For the rest, it’s a worker placement game. As there are dozens out there of those games, it has to be really good, to fulfill the expectations this game has given us. And to be honest. It didn’t fulfill our expectations. It’s nice, it’s fun, but the game relies too much on the gimmick of the turning wheels. When you don’t expect anything of this game, you might be surprised, and you’ll get a nice worker placement game. We’re pretty sure that this one goes on sales in 1 or 2 years.

RATING: 7
Another worker placement-with-a-twist, a la Stone Age or Egizia.  Place dudes to gain cubes, then place dudes to convert cubes for points. 

The twist here is the five interconnected gears.  You place on the lowest notch of a gear, and each turn you leave your guy unused he powers up a notch.

So pretty.  Love the gears and the board and the "crystal" skulls.  But DAMN, the analysis paralysis of this game...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Probably the best worker placement game I've ever played. It is incredibly thinky due to the temporal element of worker actions, yet very simple. The rules are easy to grok and the gears keep things from being fiddly. Let me be clear though, it is VERY thinky and can be hard to wrap your mind around, but that's due to the strategy and not the rules. It's also a lot more forgiving than Agricola, though it does have a similar feed your workers requirement.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
卓尔金：玛雅日历是一款普通的工人放置类游戏，但是也有其独特的地方，具体体现在其精妙的齿轮轮盘上。游戏版图上有六大轮盘，中心大轮盘代表着玛雅历法的一年一周期，大轮盘带动周边五个小轮盘转动，每转动一格表示一个时间单位过去，而五个小轮盘代表着玛雅文明五个不同的发展层面，而玩家的工人将往返于这些各具特点小轮盘，执行其提供的各种行动。游戏比较精美，规则也通俗易懂，但是在轮盘转动规律方面爱好者们需要一些时间去细细体会，可谓小游戏有大味道。。。可谓生命有限，一年时间中能完成多少事情？
【关键词】齿轮轮盘，七齿六齿，玉米，粮食日，水晶头骨，农业/采矿/建筑/宗教科技，纪念碑，三大神明，奇迹轨迹，奇琴伊察，上人/下人/乞讨

【推荐度】高【文明建设发展，中重度德式爱好者
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
